<p>INTRODUCTION /PREAMBLE</p>
<p>Epidemiology, literally meaning "the study of what is upon the people", is derived from Greek epi, meaning "upon, among", demos, meaning "people, district", and logos, meaning "study, suggesting that it applies only to human populations. However, the term is widely used in studies of zoological populations (veterinary epidemiology), "epizoology" has also been applied to studies of plant populations (botanical or plant disease epidemiology).</p>
<p>The distinction between "epidemic" and "endemic" was first drawn to distinguish between diseases that are "visited upon" a population (epidemic) from those that "reside within" a population (endemic).</p>
<p>The term "epidemiology" appears to have first been used to describe the study of epidemics. Epidemiologists also study the interaction of diseases in a population, a condition known as a syndemic.</p>
<p>The term epidemiology is now widely applied to cover the description and causation of not only epidemic diseases, but of diseases in general, and even many non-disease health-related conditions, such as high blood pressure and obesity. Therefore, epidemiology is based upon how the pattern of the disease cause changes in the function of everyone.</p>
<p>Epidemiology is defined as the study of distribution and determinants of health related states in populations and use of this study to address health related problems.</p>
<p>The practice: Epidemiologists employ a range of study designs namely:</p>
<ol>
<li>Observational</li>
<li>Experimental and generally categorized as:
a) Descriptive,
b) Analytic (aiming to further examine known associations or hypothesized relationships), and
c) Experimental (a term often equated with clinical or community trials of treatments and other interventions).</li>
</ol>
<p>In observational studies, nature is allowed to "take its course", as epidemiologists observe from the sidelines with no control of the factors.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>3</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Conversely, in experimental studies, the epidemiologist is the one in control of all of the factors entering a certain case study.</p>
<p>Epidemiological studies are aimed, where possible, at revealing unbiased relationships between exposures such as alcohol or smoking, biological agents, stress, or chemicals to mortality or morbidity.</p>
<p>The identification of causal relationships between these exposures and outcomes is an important aspect of epidemiology.</p>
<p>Modern epidemiologists use informatics (The sciences of gathering, manipulating, storing, retrieving, and classifying recorded information) as a tool.</p>
<p>Observational studies have two components:</p>
<ol>
<li>Descriptive observational study, or</li>
<li>Analytical observational study.</li>
</ol>
<p>Descriptive observations pertain to the “who, what, where and when of health-related state occurrence”.</p>
<p>Analytical observations deal more with the ‘how’ of a health-related event.</p>
<p>Experimental epidemiology contains three case types:</p>
<ol>
<li>Randomized control trial (often used for new medicine or drug testing),</li>
<li>Field trial (conducted on those at a high risk of contracting a disease), and</li>
<li>Community trial (research on social originating diseases).</li>
</ol>
<p>The term 'epidemiologic triad'(A set of three similar things considered as a unit) is used to describe the intersection (common ground) of:</p>
<ol>
<li>Host,</li>
<li>Agent, and</li>
<li>Environment in analyzing an outbreak.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-d86f7932.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="legal-interpretation">Legal interpretation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#legal-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Epidemiological studies can only go to prove that an agent could have caused, but not that it did caused, an effect in any particular case.</li>
<li>Epidemiology is concerned with the incidence of disease in populations and does not address the question of the cause of an individual's disease.</li>
<li>Epidemiology addresses whether an agent can cause a disease, not whether an agent did cause a specific plaintiff's (complainant) disease."</li>
</ol>
<p>Population-based health management: Epidemiological practice and the results of epidemiological analysis make a significant contribution to emerging population-based health management frameworks.</p>
<p>Population-based health management encompasses the ability to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Assess the health states and health needs of a target population;</li>
<li>Implement and evaluate interventions that are designed to improve the health of that population; and</li>
<li>Efficiently and effectively provide care for members of that population in a way that is consistent with the community's:
i. Cultural,
ii. Policy and
iii. Health resource values.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="population-based-health-management-framework-called-life-at-risk-combines">Population-based health management framework called Life at Risk combines:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#population-based-health-management-framework-called-life-at-risk-combines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Epidemiological quantitative analysis with demographics,</li>
<li>Health agency operational research and</li>
<li>Economics to perform:</li>
</ol>
<p>a) Population Life Impacts Simulations: Measurement of the future potential impact of disease upon the population with respect to:
i. new disease cases (incidence),
ii. prevalence,
iii. premature death as well as
iv. potential years of life lost from disability and death;</p>
<p>b) Labor Force Life Impacts Simulations: Measurement of the future potential impact of disease upon the labor force with respect to:
i. new disease cases,
ii. prevalence,
iii. premature death and
iv. potential years of life lost from disability and death;</p>

<p>c) Economic Impacts of Disease Simulations: Measurement of the future potential impact of disease upon:</p>
<p>i. Private sector disposable income impacts (wages, corporate profits, private health care costs) and
ii. Public sector disposable income impacts (personal income tax, corporate income tax, consumption taxes, publicly funded health care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="concept-and-historical-evolution-of-epidemiology">CONCEPT AND HISTORICAL EVOLUTION OF EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#concept-and-historical-evolution-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="terms-in-epidemiology">TERMS IN EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#terms-in-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Epidemiology is the study of:</li>
</ol>
<p>a) The distribution,
b) Frequency and
c) Determinants of:</p>
<ul>
<li>Health related states or</li>
<li>Events or</li>
<li>Diseases in specified populations against the background of its total environment.</li>
</ul>
<p>Epidemiology can also be defined as the study (or the science of the study) of:</p>
<ol>
<li>The patterns,</li>
<li>Causes, and</li>
<li>Effects of health and</li>
<li>Disease conditions in defined populations.</li>
</ol>
<p>It is the cornerstone of public health used in identifying:</p>
<ol>
<li>Risk factors for disease and</li>
<li>Targets for preventive medicine.</li>
</ol>
<p>Epidemiologists help with:</p>
<ol>
<li>Study design,</li>
<li>Collection and</li>
<li>Statistical analysis of data, and</li>
<li>Interpretation and</li>
<li>Dissemination of results (including peer review (Evaluate professionally a colleague's work) and occasional systematic review.</li>
</ol>
<p>Epidemiology has helped develop methodology used in:</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol>
<li>Clinical research,</li>
<li>Public health studies and,</li>
<li>To a lesser extent, basic research in the biological sciences.</li>
</ol>
<p>Major areas of epidemiological study include disease:</p>
<ol>
<li>Etiology,</li>
<li>Outbreak investigation,</li>
<li>Surveillance and screening,</li>
<li>Bio-monitoring, and</li>
<li>Comparisons of treatment effects such as in clinical trials.</li>
</ol>
<p>Epidemiologists rely on other scientific disciplines like biology to better understand:</p>
<ol>
<li>Disease processes,</li>
<li>Statistics to make efficient use of the data and draw appropriate conclusions,</li>
<li>Social sciences to better understand, proximate and distal (Situated farthest from point of attachment or origin, as of a limb or bone) causes, and engineering for exposure assessment.</li>
</ol>
<p>Key terms in this definition reflect some of the important principles of epidemiology:</p>
<p>i. Study:</p>
<p>a) Epidemiology is a scientific discipline with sound methods of scientific inquiry at its foundation.
b) Epidemiology is data driven and relies on a systematic and unbiased approach to:
i. Collection,
ii. Analysis, and
iii. Interpretation
of data</p>
<p>Basic epidemiologic methods tend to rely on careful observation of valid comparison groups to assess whether what was observed, such as the number of cases of disease in a particular area during a particular time period or the frequency of an exposure among persons with disease, differs from what might be expected.</p>
<p>Epidemiology also draws on methods from other scientific fields, including:</p>
<ol>
<li>Biostatistics and</li>
<li>Informatics,</li>
<li>Biologic,</li>
<li>Economic, and</li>
<li>Social and</li>
<li>Behavioral sciences.</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>In fact, epidemiology is often described as the basic science of public health, and for good reason.</p>
<p>First, epidemiology is a quantitative discipline that relies on a working knowledge of:</p>
<ol>
<li>Probability,</li>
<li>statistics and</li>
<li>Sound research methods.</li>
</ol>
<p>Second, epidemiology is a method of causal reasoning based on developing and testing hypotheses grounded in such scientific fields such as:</p>
<ol>
<li>Biology,</li>
<li>Behavioral sciences,</li>
<li>Physics and</li>
<li>Ergonomics to explain health related:
i. Behaviors
ii. states, and
iii. Events.</li>
</ol>
<p>However, epidemiology is an integral component of public health, providing the foundation for directing practical appropriate publication based on science and causal reasoning.</p>
<p>ii. Distribution: Epidemiology is concerned with the frequency and pattern of health events in a population.</p>
<p>iii. Frequency: refers not only to the number of health events such as the number of cases of meningitis or diabetes in a population but also to the relationship of that number to the size of the population. The resulting rate allows epidemiologists to compare disease occurrence across different populations.</p>
<p>The occurrence of disease is examined in terms of relationship or pattern between man and his total environment. These include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Place,</li>
<li>Time and</li>
<li>Person.</li>
</ol>
<p>Place pattern: include:</p>

<p>a) Geographic variations
b) Climatic zone,
c) Country,
d) Region,
e) Province,
f) District,
g) Location,
h) City wards,
i) Urban and rural areas differences and
j) Institutions and locations of work sites.</p>
<p><strong>Time pattern:</strong> may be:
a) Annual,
b) Seasonal variations,
c) Weekly,
d) Daily,
e) Hourly,
f) Weekday versus weekends,
g) Secular trends,
h) Periodic changes,
i) Any other breakdown of time that may influence disease or injury occurrence.</p>
<p><strong>Personal pattern (person pattern):</strong> This includes:
a) Demographic factors which may be related to risk of:
i. illness,
ii. injury, or
iii. disability such as:</p>

<p>a) age,
b) sex,
c) marital status, and</p>
<p>Socio-economic status, as well as behaviors and environmental exposures such as:</p>
<p>i. Education,
ii. Occupation,
iii. Social status,
iv. Nutrition,
v. Trauma, and
vi. Fatigue.</p>
<p>N/B Characterizing events by time, place, and person, are activities of descriptive epidemiology.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Determinant: This is any factor, whether</li>
</ol>
<p>i. Event,
ii. Characteristics or
iii. Other definable entity, that brings about a change in a health condition or other defined characteristic.</p>
<p>Epidemiology is also used to search for determinants which are the causes and other factors that influence the occurrence of disease and other health- events.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Population: The complete collection of individuals that have some particular characteristic(s) in common.</li>
<li>Population Risk: A defined population and comprises the number of individuals in that original population that is considered capable of acquiring the particular disease or disease characteristic.</li>
<li>Infection: The invasion of a living organism, the host, by another ling organism – the agent.</li>
<li>Infectivity: a measure of the ability of a disease agent to establish itself in the host. The term can be used qualitatively, when an agent is referred to as being low, medium or high infectivity or quantitatively. Attempts to quantify infectivity normally involve use of a statistic known as ID. This refers to the individual dose or numbers of the agent required to infect 50% of a specified population of susceptible s under controlled environmental</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>condition. Having become infected, the host may or may not become diseased, and this is again determined by a range of intrinsic determinants affecting the agent and the host. Two terms – virulence and pathogen city- are often used to describe the ability of the agent to cause disease.</p>
<ol start="8">
<li>
<p>Virulence: a measure of the severity of a disease caused by a specified agent. Virulence is a laboratory term and is used to measure the varying ability of disease agents to produce disease under controlled conditions. It is often quantified by a statistic known as <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi></mrow><mn>50</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{LD}_{50}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">LD</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">50</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> which refers to the individual dose or numbers of the agent which will kill <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>50</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">50\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">50%</span></span></span></span></span> of specified population of susceptible under controlled environmental conditions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disease: a derangement (A state of mental disturbance and disorientation) in the function of the whole body of the host or any of its parts. It also can be defined as a derangement in the function of the whole body of the host or any of its parts, or it can be defined as abnormal medical condition of an organism that impairs bodily functions associated with specific symptoms and signs. It may be external factors such as infectious disease, or it may be caused by internal dysfunction such as auto-immune disease (Of or relating to the immune response of the body against substance normally present in the body.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Physiology: The branch of the biological sciences dealing with the functioning of organisms or Processes and functions of an organism.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Determinant: Any factor or variable that can affect the frequency with which a disease occurs in a population.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Determinants can be broadly classified as being either intrinsic or extrinsic in nature.</p>
<p>a) Intrinsic Determinants of Disease: are physical or physiological characteristics of the host or disease agent [or intermediate host or vector, if present] which are generally determined genetically.</p>

<p>Disease agents as determinants of disease: Agents associated with disease can be categorized into two brad groups:</p>
<p>i. "Living" agents, such as:
a) Viruses,
b) Bacteria,
c) Rickettsia,
d) Protozoa,
e) Helminthes,
f) Protozoa etc.</p>
<p>ii. "Non-living" agents, such as:
a) Heat and
b) Cold,
c) Water,
d) Nutrients,
e) Toxic substances etc.</p>
<p>In instances of infectious diseases, the presence of the etiological agent is the main determining factor in the epidemiology of the disease. Obviously, disease cannot occur in the absence of the agent, but, conversely, disease need not always result from the presence of the agent. This leads to the important epidemiological distinction between infection and disease.</p>
<p>b) Extrinsic Determinants: are normally associated with some form of environmental influence on the host or disease agent [or intermediate host or vector, if present].</p>
<ol start="12">
<li>Health Related States or Events: is defined as states or events that affect the well-being of a population.</li>
</ol>
<p>Epidemiology was originally focused exclusively on epidemics of communicable diseases but was subsequently expanded to address endemic communicable diseases and non-communicable infectious diseases.</p>
<p>By the middle of 20th century, additional epidemiologic methods had been developed and applied in:</p>
<ol>
<li>Chronic diseases,</li>
<li>Injuries,</li>
<li>Birth defects,</li>
<li>Mental-child health,</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Occupational health, and</li>
<li>Environmental health.</li>
</ol>
<p>Then epidemiologists began looking at behavior related to health and well-being such as:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Amount of exercise, and</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use of seat-belt.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Specified Populations: Although epidemiologists and direct health-care providers are both concerned with occurrence and control of disease, they differ greatly in how they view patients. The clinician is concerned about the health of an individual; the epidemiologist is concerned about the collective health of the community or population. In other words, the clinician’s patient is an individual; the epidemiologist’s patient is the community. Therefore, the clinician and the epidemiologist have different responsibilities when faced with a person with illness. For example, when patients with diarrheal disease present, both are interested in establishing the correct diagnosis. However, while clinician usually focuses on treating and caring for the individual, the epidemiologist focuses on identifying the exposure or source that caused the illness; the number of persons who may have similarly exposed; the potential for further spread in community; and interventions to prevent additional cases or recurrences.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Application: Epidemiology is not “just the study” of health in a population; it also involves applying the knowledge gained by the studies to community-based practice. Like the practice of medicine, the practice of epidemiology is both science and art. To make the proper diagnosis and prescribe appropriate treatment for a patient, the clinician combines medical (scientific) knowledge with experience, clinical judgment, and understanding of the patient. Similarly, the epidemiologist uses the scientific methods of descriptive and analytic epidemiology as well as experience, epidemiologic judgment, and understanding of local conditions in “diagnosing” the health of a community and proposing appropriate, practical, and acceptable public health interventions to control and prevent disease in the community.</p>
<ol start="14">
<li>Health: is the level of functional or metabolic efficiency of a living organism. In humans, it is the general condition of a person's mind and body, usually meaning to be free from illness, injury or pain (as in "good health" or "healthy").</li>
</ol>
<p>The World Health Organization (WHO) defined health in its broader sense in 1946 as "a state of complete physical, mental, and social well-being and not merely the absence of disease or infirmity</p>
<p>In addition to health care interventions and a person's surroundings, a number of other factors are known to influence the health status of individuals, including their:</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol>
<li>Background,</li>
<li>Lifestyle, and</li>
<li>Economic and</li>
<li>Social conditions.</li>
</ol>
<p>These are referred to as "determinants of health."</p>
<p>Studies have shown that high levels of stress can affect your health.</p>
<p><strong>Mental health</strong>: The World Health Organization describes mental health as "a state of well-being in which the individual realizes:</p>
<ol>
<li>His or her own abilities,</li>
<li>Can cope with the normal stresses of life,</li>
<li>Can work productively and fruitfully, and</li>
<li>Is able to make a contribution to his or her community.</li>
</ol>
<p>Mental Health is not just the absence of mental illness.</p>
<p><strong>Mental illness</strong> is described as the spectrum of:</p>
<ol>
<li>Cognitive,</li>
<li>emotional, and</li>
<li>Behavioral conditions that interfere with social and emotional wellbeing and the lives and productivity of people.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Public health</strong>: Public health has been described as "the science and art of:</p>
<ol>
<li>Preventing disease,</li>
<li>prolonging life and</li>
<li>Promoting health through the organized efforts and informed choices of:
i. Society,
ii. Organizations,
iii. Public and
iv. Private,
v. Communities and individuals."</li>
</ol>
<p>It is concerned with threats to the overall health of a community based on population health analysis. The population in question can be as small as a handful of people or as large as all the inhabitants of several continents (for instance, in the case of a pandemic).</p>
<p>Public health has many sub-fields, but typically includes the interdisciplinary categories of:</p>
<ol>
<li>Epidemiology,</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Biostatistics and</li>
<li>Health services.</li>
<li>Environmental health,</li>
<li>community health,</li>
<li>behavioral health, and</li>
<li>Occupational health is also important areas of public health.</li>
</ol>
<p>The focus of public health interventions is to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Prevent and</li>
<li>Manage diseases,</li>
<li>Injuries and</li>
<li>Other health conditions through surveillance of cases and the promotion of healthy behavior,</li>
<li>Communities, and</li>
<li>(in aspects relevant to human health) environments.</li>
</ol>
<p>Its aim is to prevent health problems from happening or re-occurring by implementing:</p>
<ol>
<li>Educational programs,</li>
<li>Developing policies,</li>
<li>Administering services, and</li>
<li>Conducting research.</li>
</ol>
<p>In many cases, treating a disease or controlling a pathogen can be vital to preventing it in others, such as during an outbreak. Vaccination programs and distribution of condoms to prevent the spread of communicable diseases are examples of common preventive public health measures, as are educational campaigns to promote vaccination and the use of condoms (including overcoming resistance to such).</p>
<p>Public health also takes various actions to limit the health disparities between different areas of the country and, in some cases, the continent or world. One issue is the access of individuals and communities to:</p>
<ol>
<li>health care in terms of financial,</li>
<li>Geographical or socio-cultural constraints to accessing and using services. Applications of the public health system include the areas of:
i. Maternal and child health,
ii. Health services administration,
iii. Emergency response, and
iv. Prevention and control of infectious and chronic diseases.</li>
</ol>
<p>The great positive impact of public health programs is widely acknowledged. Due in part to the policies and actions developed through public health, the 20th century registered a decrease in</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="self-care-strategies-personal-health-depends-partially-on-the">Self-care strategies: Personal health depends partially on the:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#self-care-strategies-personal-health-depends-partially-on-the" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="these-include">These include:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#these-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Personal actions for preventing or minimizing the effects of a disease, usually a chronic condition, through integrative care.</li>

<p>The information gleaned from personal observations of daily living – such as</p>

<li>about sleep patterns,</li>
<li>exercise behavior,</li>
<li>nutritional intake, and</li>
<li>environmental features – may be used to inform personal decisions and actions (e.g., "I feel tired in the morning so I am going to try sleeping on a different pillow"), as well as clinical decisions and treatment plans (e.g., a patient who notices his or her shoes are tighter than usual may be having exacerbation of left-sided heart failure, and may require diuretic medication to reduce fluid overload).</li>
</ol>
<p>Personal health also depends partially on the social structure of a person's life.</p>
<p>The maintenance of strong social relationships, volunteering, and other social activities have been linked to positive mental health and even increased longevity.</p>
<p>One American study among seniors over age 70, found that frequent volunteering was associated with reduced risk of dying compared with older persons who did not volunteer, regardless of physical health status.</p>
<p>Another study from Singapore reported that volunteering retirees had significantly better cognitive performance scores, fewer depressive symptoms, and better mental well-being and life satisfaction than non-volunteering retirees.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Prolonged psychological stress may negatively impact health, and has been cited as a factor in cognitive impairment with:</p>
<ol>
<li>Aging,</li>
<li>Depressive illness, and</li>
<li>Expression of disease.</li>
</ol>
<p>Stress management is the application of methods to either reduce stress or increase tolerance to stress.</p>
<p>Relaxation techniques are physical methods used to relieve stress.</p>
<p>Psychological methods include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Cognitive therapy,</li>
<li>Meditation, and</li>
<li>Positive thinking, which work by reducing response to stress.</li>
</ol>
<p>Improving relevant skills, such as problem solving and time management skills, reduces uncertainty and builds confidence, which also reduces the reaction to stress-causing situations where those skills are applicable.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="environment-may-refer-to">Environment may refer to:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#environment-may-refer-to" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<p>Environmental health is that branch of public health that is concerned with all aspects of the natural and built environment that may affect human health. Other phrases that concern or refer to the discipline of environmental health include environmental public health, and</p>

<p>environmental protection. The field of environmental health is closely related to environmental science and public health as environmental health is concerned with environmental factors affecting human health.</p>
<p>"Environmental health addresses all the:</p>
<ol>
<li>physical,</li>
<li>chemical, and</li>
<li>Biological factors external to a person and all the related factors impacting behaviours.</li>
</ol>
<p>It encompasses the assessment and control of those environmental factors that can potentially affect health. It is targeted towards preventing disease and creating health-supportive environments.</p>
<p>This definition excludes behaviour not related to environment, as well as behaviour related to the social and cultural environment, as well as genetics."</p>
<p>Environmental health is defined by the World Health Organization as those aspects of the human health and disease that are determined by factors in the environment. It also refers to the theory and practice of assessing and controlling factors in the environment that can potentially affect health.</p>
<p>Environmental health services are defined by the World Health Organization as:</p>
<p>vi. Those services which implement environmental health policies through monitoring and control activities. They also carry out that role by promoting the improvement of environmental parameters and by encouraging the use of environmentally friendly and healthy technologies and behaviors. They also have a leading role in developing and suggesting new policy areas.</p>
<p>vii. Environmental medicine may be seen as the medical branch of the broader field of environmental health. Terminology is not fully established, and in many European countries they are used interchangeably.</p>
<p>Environmental health profession: Environmental health professionals may be known as</p>
<ol>
<li>Environmental health officers,</li>
<li>Environmental protection officers,</li>
<li>Public health inspectors,</li>
<li>Environmental health specialists,</li>
<li>Environmental health practitioners,</li>
</ol>

<ol start="6">
<li>Sanitarians, environmental inspectors, or</li>
<li>Environmental specialists.</li>
</ol>
<p>"Scope of practice in environmental health" means the practice of environmental health by registered environmental health specialists in the public and private sector and includes, but is not limited to:</p>
<ol>
<li>organization,</li>
<li>management,</li>
<li>education,</li>
<li>enforcement,</li>

<p>i. food protection;

xv. milk and dairy</p>
<p>The environmental health profession had its modern-day roots in the sanitary and public health movement of the United Kingdom. This was epitomized by Sir Edwin Chadwick, who was instrumental in the repeal of the poor laws and was the founding president of the Association of Public Sanitary Inspectors in 1884, which today is the Chartered Institute of Environmental Health.</p>
<p>Disciplines: Three basic disciplines generally contribute to the field of environmental health:</p>
<ol>
<li>Environmental epidemiology,</li>
<li>Toxicology, and</li>
<li>Exposure science.</li>
</ol>
<p>Each of these disciplines contributes different information to describe problems in environmental health, but there is some overlap among them.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>19</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Environmental epidemiology studies the relationship between environmental exposures (including exposure to chemicals, radiation, microbiological agents, etc.) and human health. Observational studies, which simply observe exposures that people have already experienced, are common in environmental epidemiology because humans cannot ethically be exposed to agents that are known or suspected to cause disease.</p>
<p>While the inability to use experimental study designs is a limitation of environmental epidemiology, this discipline directly observes effects on human health</p>
<p>Toxicology studies show how environmental exposures lead to specific health outcomes, generally in animals, as a means to understand possible health outcomes in humans.</p>
<p>Toxicology has the advantage of being able to conduct randomized controlled trials and other experimental studies because they can use animal subjects. However there are many differences in animal and human biology, and there can be a lot of uncertainty when interpreting the results of animal studies for their implications for human health.</p>
<p>Exposure science studies human exposure to environmental contaminants by both identifying and quantifying exposures.</p>
<p>Exposure science can be used to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Support environmental epidemiology by better describing environmental exposures that may lead to a particular health outcome,</li>
<li>Identify common exposures whose health outcomes may be better understood Through a toxicology study, or</li>
<li>Can be used in a risk assessment to determine whether current levels of exposure might exceed recommended levels.</li>
</ol>
<p>Exposure science has the advantage of being able to very accurately quantify exposures to specific chemicals, but it does not generate any information about health outcomes like environmental epidemiology or toxicology.</p>
<p>Information from these three disciplines can be combined to conduct a risk assessment for specific chemicals or mixtures of chemicals to determine whether an exposure poses significant risk to human health. This can in turn be used to develop and implement environmental health policy that, for example, regulates chemical emissions, or imposes standards for proper sanitation.</p>
<p>Concerns: Environmental health addresses all human-health-related aspects of both the natural environment and the built environment.</p>
<p>Environmental health concerns include:</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol>
<li>Air quality, including both ambient outdoor air and indoor air quality, which also comprises concerns about environmental tobacco smoke.</li>
<li>Body art safety, including tattooing, body piercing and permanent cosmetics.</li>
<li>Climate change and its effects on health.</li>
<li>Disaster preparedness and response.</li>
<li>Food safety, including in agriculture, transportation, food processing, wholesale and retail distribution and sale.</li>
<li>Hazardous materials management, including hazardous waste management, contaminated site remediation, the prevention of leaks from underground storage tanks and the prevention of hazardous materials releases to the environment and responses to emergency situations resulting from such releases.</li>
<li>Housing, including substandard housing abatement and the inspection of jails and prisons.</li>
<li>Childhood lead poisoning prevention.</li>
<li>Land use planning, including smart growth.</li>
<li>Liquid waste disposal, including city waste water treatment plants and on-site waste water disposal systems, such as septic tank systems and chemical toilets.</li>
<li>Medical waste management and disposal.</li>
<li>Noise pollution control.</li>
<li>Occupational health and industrial hygiene.</li>
<li>Radiological health, including exposure to ionizing radiation from X-rays or radioactive isotopes.</li>
<li>Recreational water illness prevention, including from swimming pools, spas and ocean and freshwater bathing places.</li>
<li>Safe drinking water.</li>
<li>Solid waste management, including landfills, recycling facilities, composting and solid waste transfer stations.</li>
<li>Toxic chemical exposure whether in consumer products, housing, workplaces, air, water or soil.</li>
<li>Vector control, including the control of mosquitoes, rodents, flies, cockroaches and other animals that may transmit pathogens.</li>
</ol>
<p>Prevalence: In epidemiology, the prevalence or prevalence proportion is the proportion of a population found to have a condition (typically a disease or a risk factor such as smoking or seatbelt use).</p>
<p>It is arrived at by comparing the number of people found to have the condition with the total number of people studied, and is usually expressed as a fraction, as a percentage or as the number of cases per 10,000 or 100,000 people.</p>
<p>"Point prevalence" is the proportion of a population that has the condition at a specific point in time.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth
This document is available on
8
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>"Period prevalence" is the proportion of a population that has the condition at some time during a given period ("12-month prevalence", etc.), and includes people who already have the condition at the start of the study period as well as those who acquire it during that period.</p>
<p>"Lifetime prevalence" (LTP) is the proportion of a population that at some point in their life (up to the time of assessment) have experienced the condition.</p>
<p>Prevalence estimates are used by epidemiologists, health care providers, government agencies, and insurers.</p>
<p>Prevalence is contrasted with incidence, which is a measure of new cases arising in a population over a given period (month, year, etc.). For memory purposes, the difference between prevalence and incidence can be summarized thus:</p>
<p>That prevalence answers "How many people have this disease right now?" and</p>
<p>That incidence answers "How many people per year newly acquire this disease?"</p>
<p>In science, prevalence describes a proportion (typically expressed as a percentage). For example, the prevalence of obesity among American adults in 2001 was estimated by the U. S. Centers for Disease Control (CDC) at approximately 20.9%.</p>
<p>Prevalence is a term which means being widespread and it is distinct from incidence. Prevalence is a measurement of all individuals affected by the disease at a particular time, whereas incidence is a measurement of the number of new individuals who contract a disease during a particular period of time.</p>
<p>To illustrate, a long term disease that was spread widely in a community in 2002 will have a high prevalence at a given point of 2003 (assuming it has a long duration) but it might have a low incidence rate during 2003 (i.e. lots of existing cases, but not many new ones in that year). Conversely, a disease that is easily transmitted but has a short duration might spread widely during 2002 but is likely to have a low prevalence at any given point in 2003 (due to its short duration) but a high incidence during 2002 (as many people develop the disease). As such, prevalence is a useful parameter when talking about long lasting diseases, such as HIV, but incidence is more useful when talking about diseases of short duration, such as chickenpox.</p>
<p>Uses: include the following:</p>
<ol>
<li>Lifetime prevalence: Lifetime prevalence (LTP) is the number of individuals in a statistical population that at some point in their life (up to the time of assessment) have experienced a "case" (e.g., a disorder), compared to the total number of individuals (i.e. it is expressed as a ratio or percentage). Often, 12-month prevalence (or some other type of "period prevalence") is used in conjunction with lifetime prevalence.</li>
</ol>

<li>
<p>There is also point prevalence, the prevalence of disorder at a more specific (a month or less) point in time. There is also a related figure lifetime morbid risk - the theoretical prevalence at any point in life for anyone, regardless of time of assessment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Period prevalence: In epidemiology, Period prevalence is the proportion of the population with a given disease or condition over a specific period of time. It could describe how many people in a population had a cold over the cold season in 2006, for example. It is expressed as a percentage of the population and can be described by the following formula:</p>
</li>
</ol>

<p>The relationship between incidence (rate), point prevalence (ratio) and period prevalence (ratio) is easily explicated via an analogy with photography. Point prevalence is akin to a flash lit photograph: what is happening at this instant frozen in time. Period prevalence is analogous to a long exposure (seconds, rather than an instant) photograph: the number of events recorded in the photo whilst the camera shutter was open. In a movie each frame records an instant (point prevalence); by looking from frame to frame one notice new events (incident events) and can relate the number of such events to a period (number of frames).</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>Point prevalence: In epidemiology, point prevalence is a measure of the proportion of people in a population who have a disease or condition at a particular time, such as a particular date. It is like a snap shot of the disease in time. It can be used for statistics on the occurrence of chronic diseases. This is in contrast to period prevalence which is a measure of the proportion of people in a population who have a disease or condition over a specific period of time, say a season, or a year. Point prevalence can be described by the formula: Prevalence = Number of existing cases on a specific date ÷ Number of people in the population on this date</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Limitations: It can be said that a very small error applied over a very large number of individuals (that is, those who are not affected by the condition in the general population during their lifetime; for example, over 95%) produces a relevant, non-negligible number of subjects who are incorrectly classified as having the condition or any other condition which is the object of a survey study: these subjects are the so-called false positives; such reasoning applies to the 'false positive' but not the 'false negative' problem where we have an error applied over a relatively very small number of individuals to begin with (that is, those who are</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>affected by the condition in the general population; for example, less than 5%). Hence, a very high percentage of subjects who seem to have a history of a disorder at interview are false positives for such a medical condition and apparently never suffered a fully clinical syndrome.</p>
<p><strong>Incidence</strong>: Incidence is the number of:</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>New cases or a condition,</li>
<li>Symptom,</li>
<li>Death, or</li>
<li>Injury that develop during a specific time period, such as a year.</li>
</ol>
<p>The number is often expressed as a percentage of a population. For example, 25% of Americans were diagnosed with the flu in 2002.</p>
<p>Incidence shows the likelihood that a person in that population will be affected by the condition.</p>
<ol start="8">
<li>
<p><strong>Incidence</strong> is a measure of the <strong>risk</strong> of developing some new condition within a specified period of time. Although sometimes loosely expressed simply as the number of new cases during some time period, it is better expressed as a proportion or a rate with a denominator.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Incidence proportion</strong> (also known as cumulative incidence)</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mtext>The&nbsp;number&nbsp;of&nbsp;new&nbsp;cases&nbsp;within&nbsp;a&nbsp;specified&nbsp;time&nbsp;period</mtext><mo>×</mo><mn>1000</mn></mrow><mtext>The&nbsp;size&nbsp;of&nbsp;the&nbsp;population&nbsp;initially&nbsp;at&nbsp;risk.</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{\text{The number of new cases within a specified time period} \times 1000}{\text{The size of the population initially at risk.}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.2519em; <div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mn>28</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>100</mn><mo>=</mo><mn>2.8</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">28 \times 100 = 2.8\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">28</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">100</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">2.8%</span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>1000</p>
<p>For example, if a population initially contains 1,000 non-diseased persons and 28 develop a condition over two years of observation, the incidence proportion is 28 cases per 1,000 persons, i.e. 2.8%.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="10">
<li>Incidence rate: The incidence rate is the number of new cases per population at risk in a given time period.</li>
</ol>
<p>When the denominator is the sum of the person-time of the at risk population, it is also known as the incidence density rate or person-time incidence rate.</p>
<p>In the same example as above, the incidence rate is 14 cases per 1000 person-years, because the incidence proportion (28 per 1,000) is divided by the number of years (two). Using person-time rather than just time handles situations where the amount of observation time differs between people, or when the population at risk varies with time. Use of this measure implicitly implies the assumption that the incidence rate is constant over different periods of time, such that for an incidence rate of 14 per 1000 persons-years, 14 cases would be expected for 1000 persons observed for 1 year or 50 persons observed for 20 years.</p>
<ol start="11">
<li>Incidence vs. prevalence: Incidence should not be confused with prevalence, which is the proportion of cases in the population at a given time rather than rate of occurrence of new cases. Thus, incidence conveys information about the risk of contracting the disease, whereas prevalence indicates how widespread the disease is. Prevalence is the proportion of the total number of cases to the total population and is more a measure of the burden of the disease on society with no regard to time at risk or when subjects may have been exposed to a possible risk factor. Prevalence can also be measured with respect to a specific subgroup of a population (see: denominator data). Incidence is usually more useful than prevalence in understanding the disease etiology: for example, if the incidence rate population of a disease increases, then there is a risk factor that promotes the incidence.</li>
</ol>
<p>For example, consider a disease that takes a long time to cure and was widespread in 2002 but dissipated in 2003. This disease will have both high incidence and high prevalence in 2002, but in 2003 it will have a low incidence yet will continue to have a high prevalence (because it takes a long time to cure, so the fraction of affected individuals remains high). In contrast, a disease that has a short duration may have a low prevalence and a high incidence. When the incidence is approximately constant for the duration of the disease, prevalence is approximately the product of disease incidence and average disease duration, so <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>p</mi><mi>r</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>v</mi><mi>a</mi><mi>l</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>c</mi><mi>e</mi><mo>=</mo><mi>i</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>c</mi><mi>i</mi><mi>d</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>c</mi><mi>e</mi><mo>×</mo><mi>d</mi><mi>u</mi><mi>r</mi><mi>a</mi><mi>t</mi><mi>i</mi><mi>o</mi><mi>n</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">prevalence = incidence \times duration</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">p</span><span class="mord mathnormal">re</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.03588em;">v</span><span class="mord mathnormal">a</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.01968em;">l</span><span class="mord mathnormal">e</span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span><span class="mord mathnormal">ce</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7778em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">in</span><span class="mord mathnormal">c</span><span class="mord mathnormal">i</span><span class="mord mathnormal">d</span><span class="mord mathnormal">e</span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span><span class="mord mathnormal">ce</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">d</span><span class="mord mathnormal">u</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">r</span><span class="mord mathnormal">a</span><span class="mord mathnormal">t</span><span class="mord mathnormal">i</span><span class="mord mathnormal">o</span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span></span></span></span></span>. The importance of this equation is in the relation between prevalence and incidence; for example, when the incidence increases, then the prevalence must also increase. Note that this relation does not hold for age-specific prevalence and incidence, where the relation becomes more complicated.</p>
<ol start="12">
<li>Epidemic: In epidemiology, an epidemic (from ὄπι (epi), meaning "upon or above" and δήμος (demos), meaning "people") occurs when new cases of a certain</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>0</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>disease, in a given human population, and during a given period, substantially exceed what is expected based on recent experience. Epidemiologists often consider the term outbreak to be synonymous to epidemic, but the general public typically perceives outbreaks to be more local and less serious than epidemics.</p>
<p>Epidemics of infectious disease are generally caused by a change in the ecology of the host population (e.g. increased stress or increase in the density of a vector species), a genetic change in the parasite population or the introduction of a new parasite to a host population (by movement of parasites or hosts). Generally, an epidemic occurs when host immunity to a parasite population is suddenly reduced below that found in the endemic equilibrium and the transmission threshold is exceeded.</p>
<p>An epidemic may be restricted to one location; however, if it spreads to other countries or continents and affects a substantial number of people, it may be termed a pandemic. The declaration of an epidemic usually requires a good understanding of a baseline rate of incidence; epidemics for certain diseases, such as influenza, are defined as reaching some defined increase in incidence above this baseline. A few cases of a very rare disease may be classified as an epidemic, while many cases of a common disease.</p>
<p><strong>Causes:</strong> There are several changes that may occur in an infectious agent that may trigger an epidemic these included.</p>
<ol>
<li>Increased virulence</li>
<li>Introduction into a novel setting</li>
<li>Changes in host susceptibility to the infectious agent</li>
</ol>
<p>An epidemic disease is not required to be contagious, and the term has been applied to West Nile fever and the obesity epidemic, among others.</p>
<p>The conditions which govern the outbreak of epidemics include infected food supplies such as contaminated drinking water and the migration of populations of certain animals, such as rats or mosquitoes, which can act as disease vectors. Certain epidemics occur at certain seasons: for example, whooping-cough occurs in spring, whereas measles produces two epidemics, one in winter and one in March. Influenza, the common cold, and other infections of the upper respiratory tract, such as sore throat, occur predominantly in the winter. There is another variation, both as regards the number of persons affected and the number who die in successive epidemics: the severity of successive epidemics rises and falls over periods of five or ten years.</p>
<p><strong>Types:</strong></p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Common source outbreak:</strong> In a common source outbreak, the affected individuals had an exposure to a common agent. If the exposure is singular and all of the affected individuals develop the disease over a single</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>exposure and incubation course, it can be termed a point source outbreak. If the exposure was continuous or variable, it can be termed a continuous outbreak or intermittent outbreak, respectively.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Propagated outbreak: In a propagated outbreak, the disease spreads person-to-person. Affected individuals may become independent reservoirs leading to further exposures.</li>
</ol>
<p>Many epidemics will have characteristics of both common source and propagated outbreaks. For example, secondary person-to-person spread may occur after a common source exposure or an environmental vector may spread a zoonotic diseases agent.</p>
<p><strong>Transmission:</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Airborne transmission: Airborne transmission is the spread of infection by droplet nuclei or dust in the air. Without the intervention of winds or drafts the distance over which airborne infection takes place is short, say 10 to 20 feet.</li>
<li>Arthropod transmission: Arthropod transmission takes place by an insect, either mechanically through a contaminated proboscis or feet, or biologically when there is growth or replication of an organism in the arthropod.</li>
<li>Biological transmission: Involving a biological process, e.g. passing a stage of development of the infecting agent in an intermediate host. Opposite to mechanical transmission.</li>
<li>Colostral transmission: A form of vertical transmission via successive generations.</li>
<li>Contact transmission: The disease agent is transferred directly by biting, sucking, chewing or indirectly by inhalation of droplets, drinking of contaminated water, traveling in contaminated vehicles.</li>
<li>Cyclopropagative transmission: The agent undergoes both development and multiplication in the transmitting vehicle.</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>0</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>
<p>Developmental transmission: The agent undergoes some development in the transmission vehicle.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Fecal-oral transmission: The infectious agent is shed by the infected host in feces and acquired by the susceptible host through ingestion of contained material.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Horizontal transmission: Lateral spread to others in the same group and at the same time; spread to contemporaries.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Mechanical transmission: The transmitter is not infected in that tissues are not invaded and the agent does not multiply.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Propagative transmission: The agent multiplies in the transmission vehicle.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Vertical transmission: From one generation to the next, perhaps transovarially or by intrauterine infection of the fetus. Some retroviruses are transmitted in the germ line, i.e. their genetic material is integrated into the DNA of either the ovum or sperm.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Pandemic: The word pandemic comes from the Greek pandemos meaning "pertaining to all people". The Greek word pan means "all" and the Greek word demos means "people". According to the Medilexicon's medical dictionary a pandemic is "Denoting a disease affecting or attacking the population of an extensive region, country, continent, global; extensively epidemic."</p>
<p>A pandemic is an outbreak of global proportions. It happens when a novel virus emerges among humans - it causes serious illness and is easily human transmissible (spreads easily from person-to-person).</p>
<p>A pandemic is an epidemic of infectious disease that has spread through human populations across a large region; for instance multiple continents, or even worldwide. A widespread endemic disease that is stable in terms of how many people are getting sick from it is not a pandemic. Further, flu pandemics generally exclude recurrences of seasonal flu. Throughout history there have been a number of pandemics, such as smallpox and tuberculosis. More recent pandemics include the HIV pandemic as well as the 1918 and 2009 H1N1 pandemics.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Definition and stages: A pandemic is an epidemic occurring on a scale which crosses international boundaries, usually affecting a large number of people.</p>
<p>A disease or condition is not a pandemic merely because it is widespread or kills many people; it must also be infectious. For instance, cancer is responsible for many deaths but is not considered a pandemic because the disease is not infectious or contagious.</p>
<p>In a virtual press conference in May 2009 on the influenza pandemic, Dr Keiji Fukuda, Assistant Director-General ad Interim for Health Security and Environment, WHO said "An easy way to think about pandemic ... is to say: a pandemic is a global outbreak. Then you might ask yourself: "What is a global outbreak"? Global outbreak means that we see both spread of the agent ... and then we see disease activities in addition to the spread of the virus."</p>
<p>Current pandemics: HIV and AIDS: HIV spread to the United States and much of the rest of the world beginning around 1969. HIV, the virus that causes AIDS, is currently a pandemic, with infection rates as high as 25% in southern and eastern Africa. In 2006 the HIV prevalence rate among pregnant women in South Africa was 29.1%. Effective education about safer sexual practices and blood borne infection precautions training have helped to slow down infection rates in several African countries sponsoring national education programs. Infection rates are rising again in Asia and the Americas. AIDS could kill 31 million people in India and 18 million in China by 2025, according to projections by U.N. population researchers. AIDS death toll in Africa may reach 90–100 million by 2025.</p>
<p>Pandemics and notable epidemics through history: There have been a number of significant pandemics recorded in human history, generally zoonoses which came about with domestication of animals, such as influenza and tuberculosis. There have been a number of particularly significant epidemics that deserve mention above the "mere" destruction of cities:</p>
<p>What is the difference between a pandemic and an epidemic? : A pandemic is different from an epidemic or seasonal outbreak.</p>
<ol>
<li>Put simply, a pandemic covers a much wider geographical area, often worldwide. A pandemic also infects many more people than an epidemic. An epidemic is specific to one city, region or country, while a pandemic goes much further than national borders.</li>
<li>An epidemic is when the number of people who become infected rises well beyond what is expected within a country or a part of a country. When the infection takes place in several countries at the same time it then starts turning into a pandemic.</li>
<li>A pandemic is usually caused by a new virus strain or subtype - a virus human either have no immunity against, or very little immunity. If immunity is low or non-existent the</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>virus is much more likely to spread around the world if it becomes easily human transmissible.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>In the case of influenza, seasonal outbreaks (epidemics) are generally caused by subtypes of a virus that is already circulating among people. Pandemics, on the other hand, are generally caused by novel subtypes - these subtypes have not circulated among people before. Pandemics can also be caused by viruses, in the case of influenza, that perhaps have not circulated among people for a very long time.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pandemics generally cause much higher numbers of deaths than epidemics. The social disruption, economic loss, and general hardship caused by a pandemic are much higher than what an epidemic can cause.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>What are the Six Stages of a pandemic?</strong> The World Health Organization has a Six Stage influenza program, plus two Periods:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p><strong>Stage 1</strong>: No animal influenza virus circulating among animals have been reported to cause infection in humans.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Stage 2</strong>: An animal influenza virus circulating in domesticated or wild animals is known to have caused infection in humans and is therefore considered a specific potential pandemic threat.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Stage 3</strong>: An animal or human-animal influenza reassortant virus has caused sporadic cases or small clusters of disease in people, but has not resulted in human-to-human transmission sufficient to sustain community-level outbreaks.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Stage 4</strong>: Human-to-human transmission of an animal or human-animal influenza reassortant virus able to sustain community-level outbreaks has been verified.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Stage 5</strong>: The same identified virus has caused sustained community level outbreaks in two or more countries in one WHO region.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>
<p>Phase 6: In addition to the criteria defined in Phase 5, the same virus has caused sustained community level outbreaks in at least one other country in another WHO region.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Lost Peak Period: Levels of pandemic influenza in most countries with adequate surveillance have dropped below peak levels.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Post Pandemic Period: Levels of influenza activity have returned to the levels seen for seasonal influenza in most countries with adequate surveillance.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>According to the WHO, if an influenza pandemic were to emerge today, we could expect:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>As people today are highly internationally mobile, the pandemic virus would spread rapidly around the world.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Vaccines, antiviral agents, and antibiotics to treat secondary infections would rapidly be in short supply</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Several months would be needed before any vaccine became available. This is because pandemic viruses are new ones.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Medical facilities would be overwhelmed</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>There would be sudden and potentially considerable shortages of personnel to provide vital community services as the illness became widespread.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Holoendemic: A disease is holoendemic when essentially every individual in a population is infected. As the disease is ubiquitous, the young are more likely to express pathogenic responses,</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>whilst the older hosts will carry the disease asymptotically, or with reduced damage, due to adaptive immunity. Examples of this holoendemic pattern are seen with malaria in areas of sub-saharan Africa (where 75% of the deaths are in children under 5) and trachoma in areas of Saudi-Arabia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="basic-principles-of-epidemiology">BASIC PRINCIPLES OF EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#basic-principles-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Epidemiology is the study of the distribution and determinants and frequency of health-related states or events in specified populations, and the application of this study to the control of health problems.</p>
<p>Every public health worker should be familiar with the basic principles in this definition and how they are useful.</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Distribution - Epidemiology is concerned with the frequency and pattern of health events in a population. Frequency includes not only the number of events in a population, but also the rate or risk of disease in the population. Determining the rate of disease occurrences (number of events divided by size of the population) is critical for making valid comparisons across different populations.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Determinants - Epidemiology is also used to search for causes and other factors that influence the occurrence of health-related events. The occurrence of a health-related event is usually related to multiple determinants that should be considered.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Examples of determinants include:</p>
<p>i. Host susceptibility to a disease, and
ii. Opportunity for exposure to a microorganism,
iii. Environmental toxin,
iv. Insect vector or
v. Other infected individual that may pose a risk for acquiring disease.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Specified populations - Epidemiologists are concerned with the collective health of people in a community or other area and the impact of health events on that population.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Application - Epidemiology provides data for directing public health action.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>An epidemiologist uses the scientific methods of descriptive and analytic epidemiology in "diagnosing" the health of a community.</p>
<p>Disease surveillance usually begins with descriptive epidemiology — defining the:</p>
<p>i. What,
ii. Who,
iii. When and
iv. Where of health-related events.</p>
<ol>
<li>What - Define the disease events and/or its determinants</li>
<li>Who - Descriptions of demographic characteristics are helpful in determining which groups are at risk for some outcome?</li>
</ol>
<p>The demographic characteristics usually include:</p>
<p>i. Age,
ii. Sex and
iii. Race/ethnicity.</p>
<p>Other categories include:</p>
<p>a) Socioeconomic status,
b) History of occupation, or
c) Smoking habits, which provide useful information about exposures that, may present a risk.</p>
<p>A history of underlying diseases may be useful for determining susceptibility to certain conditions.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>When - Knowledge of the seasonality of certain diseases helps identify unusual occurrences that may define epidemics.</li>
</ol>
<p>Temporal associations between particular exposures on illness give information about incubation periods and exposures posing a risk to others.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth
This document is available on
33
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Where - Insight into the geographical extent of health-related events gives an idea of where the agent that causes a disease normally lives and multiplies, what may carry or transmit it and how it spreads.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="uses-of-epidemiology-in-public-health">Uses of Epidemiology in Public Health<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uses-of-epidemiology-in-public-health" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>It is used in the investigation of:
i. Diseases – communicable and non-communicable.
ii. Problems.
iii. Situations, and
iv. Events that have affected or are affecting or will be affecting the health of the organism in our environment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is a basic tool for looking for signs and symptoms in the health of a community.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to:
i. Elucidate [make clear and (more) comprehensible] causal mechanism,
ii. explain local health characteristics of disease occurrence, and
iii. For administrative guidance in the provision of health services.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used on preventive interventions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to determine the fertility of a population.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can determine the number of accidents and their causes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to study the health history of a population.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to diagnose the health of a community and measures of ill-health in terms of:
i. Incidence,
ii. Prevalence, and
iii. Mortality from a defined disease.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to study the working and utilization of health services.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="10">
<li>
<p>It can be used to estimate the group experience; what the individual’s risk on the average of disease incidence and defects.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to complete a clinical picture of a chronic disease and describe its natural history.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to identify syndromes [pattern of symptoms indicative of some disease] by describing:
i. The distribution,
ii. Association, and
iii. Disassociation of phenomena [any remarkable development] on the population.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to search for cases of health and disease by studying the incidence in different groups to estimate the relative importance of different causes in multiple etiologies.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used as a strategy [an elaborate and systematic plan] of action for studying man and his total environment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to develop knowledge about both the earliest and beginnings of dysfunction and characteristics of individuals within the groups that predispose them to futuristic problems.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to develop knowledge about both the earliest and beginnings of dysfunction and characteristics of individuals within the groups that predispose them to futuristic problems.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is used to make a community diagnosis, so that you identify and describe health problems in a community. This will be a cross-sectional study since observations are made at a specific time e.g.
i. Prevalence anemia.
ii. Weight for height profile of children.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is used to monitor continuously over a period of time the change of health in a community. This will be a longitudinal as it involves the collection of information over a period of time and will commonly be related to measures which have been taken e.g. effect of program of vaccination, health, education, nutritional supplementation, safe water supply and sanitation etc.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth
This document is available on
