studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="19">
<li>
<p>It is used to practice surveillance for specific diseases e.g. cholera in order to act quickly and cut short any out-break.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is used to investigate an out-break of communicable disease, analyze the reasons for it, plan a feasible remedy and carry out and monitor the effects of the remedy on the out-break.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is used to plan effective health services.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Note: Epidemiology is about information - the information needed for health planning and supervision and evaluation of health promotion and disease control activities.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="determinants-of-disease">DETERMINANTS OF DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#determinants-of-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The determinants explored below are organized into four categories, based upon a model developed by Murray &amp; Lopez (see figure below)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Social/Environmental/Political Determinants</th><th>Behavioral/Individual Determinants</th><th>Clinical/Physiological Determinants</th><th>Health Outcomes [</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>physical environment (transport)</td><td>alcohol</td><td>heart disease</td><td>ischemic heart disease*</td></tr><tr><td>social inequality (low SES)</td><td>firearms/violence</td><td>high blood pressure</td><td>hiv/aids</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>illicit drugs</td><td>diabetes (type 2)</td><td>lung cancer stroke* depression*</td></tr><tr><td>social exclusion (institutional racism)</td><td>motor vehicle traffic</td><td>high cholesterol</td><td>suicide</td></tr><tr><td>social support (lack of social connectedness)</td><td>physical inactivity</td><td>overweight/obesity</td><td>breast cancer</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>poor diet</td><td>asthma</td><td>poisonings (overdose)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>tobacco</td><td>hiv/aids</td><td>traffic accidents</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>unsafe sex</td><td>depression</td><td>drug overdose</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>stress</td><td>malnutrition</td><td>hypertensive heart disease</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>substance use problems</td><td>colorectal cancer</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>COPD</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>violence (homicide)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>Alzheimers/dementia osteoarthritis</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>alcohol use disorder</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>colorectal cancer</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>diabetes</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>cirrhosis of liver</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>sexually transmitted disease</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>tuberculosis</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p>Determinants of health: Generally, the context in which an individual lives is of great importance for his health status and quality of life. It is increasingly recognized that health is maintained and improved not only through the advancement and application of health science, but also through the efforts and intelligent lifestyle choices of the individual and society. According to the World Health Organization, the main determinants of health include:</p>
<ol>
<li>The social and economic environment,</li>
<li>The physical environment, and</li>
<li>The person's individual characteristics and behaviors.</li>
</ol>
<p>More specifically, key factors that have been found to influence whether people are healthy or unhealthy include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Income and social status</li>
<li>Social support networks</li>
<li>Education and literacy</li>
<li>Employment/working conditions</li>
<li>Social environments</li>
<li>Physical environments</li>
<li>Personal health practices and coping skills</li>
<li>Healthy child development</li>
<li>Biology and genetics</li>
<li>Health care services</li>
<li>Gender</li>
<li>Culture</li>
</ol>
<p>An increasing number of studies and reports from different organizations and contexts examine the linkages between health and different factors, including:</p>
<ol>
<li>lifestyles,</li>
<li>environments,</li>
<li>health care organization, and</li>
</ol>
<p>37</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>health policy, and the series of World Health Reports of the World Health Organization, which focuses on global health issues including access to health care and improving public health outcomes, especially in developing countries.</li>
</ol>
<p>The concept of the "health field," as distinct from medical care, emerged from the Lalonde report from Canada. The report identified three interdependent fields as key determinants of an individual's health. These are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Lifestyle: The aggregation of personal decisions (i.e., over which the individual has control) that can be said to contribute to, or cause, illness or death;</li>
<li>Environmental: All matters related to health external to the human body and over which the individual has little or no control;</li>
<li>Biomedical: All aspects of health, physical and mental, developed within the human body as influenced by genetic make-up.</li>
</ol>
<p>The maintenance and promotion of health is achieved through different combination of:</p>
<ol>
<li>Physical,</li>
<li>Mental, and</li>
<li>Social well-being, together sometimes referred to as the "health triangle."</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-6f3039a0.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The WHO's 1986 Ottawa Charter for Health Promotion further stated that health is not just a state, but also "a resource for everyday life, not the objective of living.</p>
<p>Health is a positive concept emphasizing social and personal resources, as well as physical capacities."</p>
<p>Focusing more on lifestyle issues and their relationships with functional health, data from the Alameda County Study suggested that people can improve their health via:</p>
<ol>
<li>Exercise,</li>
<li>Enough sleep,</li>
<li>Maintaining a healthy body weight,</li>
<li>Limiting alcohol use, and</li>
<li>Avoiding smoking.</li>
</ol>
<p>The ability to adapt and to self manage has been suggested as core components of human health.</p>
<p>The environment is often cited as an important factor influencing the health status of individuals. This includes characteristics of:</p>
<ol>
<li>The natural environment,</li>
<li>The built environment, and</li>
<li>The social environment.</li>
</ol>
<p>Factors such as:</p>
<ol>
<li>clean water and</li>
<li>air,</li>
<li>adequate housing, and</li>
<li>safe communities and</li>
<li>Roads all have been found to contribute to good health, especially to the health of infants and children.</li>
</ol>
<p>Some studies have shown that a lack of neighborhood recreational spaces including natural environment leads to lower levels of personal satisfaction and higher levels of obesity, linked to lower overall health and well being. This suggests that the positive health benefits of natural space in urban neighborhoods should be taken into account in public policy and land use.</p>
<p>Genetics, or inherited traits from parents, also play a role in determining the health status of individuals and populations. This can encompass both the predisposition to certain diseases and health conditions, as well as the habits and behaviors individuals develop through the lifestyle of their families.</p>
<p>For example, genetics may play a role in the manner in which people cope with:</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol>
<li>Stress,</li>
<li>Mental,</li>
<li>Emotional or</li>
<li>Physical.</li>
</ol>
<p>Social determinants of health: are the economic and social conditions – and their distribution among the population – that influence individual and group differences in health status.</p>
<p>They are risk factors found in one's living and working conditions (such as the distribution of income, wealth, influence, and power), rather than individual factors (such as behavioural risk factors or genetics) that influence the risk for a disease, or vulnerability to disease or injury. According to some viewpoints, these distributions of social determinants are shaped by public policies that reflect the influence of prevailing political ideologies of those governing a jurisdiction. The World Health Organization says that “This unequal distribution of health-damaging experiences is not in any sense a ‘natural’ phenomenon but is the result of a toxic combination of poor social policies, unfair economic arrangements [where the already well-off and healthy become even richer and the poor who are already more likely to be ill become even poorer], and bad politics</p>
<p>Commonly accepted social determinants of health: There is no single definition of the social determinants of health, but there are commonalities, and many governmental and non-governmental organizations recognize that there are social factors which impact the health of individuals.</p>
<p>In 2003, the World Health Organization (WHO) Europe suggested that the social determinants of health included:</p>
<ol>
<li>Social gradients (life expectancy is shorter and disease is more common further down the social ladder)</li>
<li>Stress (including stress in the workplace)</li>
<li>Early childhood development</li>
<li>Social exclusion</li>
<li>Unemployment</li>
<li>Social support networks</li>
<li>Addiction</li>
<li>Availability of healthy food</li>
<li>Availability of healthy transportation</li>
</ol>
<p>The WHO later developed a Commission on Social Determinants of Health, which in 2008 published a report entitled "Closing the Gap in a Generation." This report identified two broad areas of social determinants of health that needed to be addressed.</p>
<ol>
<li>The first area was daily living conditions, which included:
a) Healthy physical environments,
b) Fair employment and decent work,</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>c) Social protection across the lifespan, and
d) Access to health care.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>The second major area was distribution of:
b) power,
c) money, and
d) resources,
e) including equity in health programs,
f) public financing of action on the social determinants,
g) economic inequalities,
h) resource depletion,
i) healthy working conditions,
j) gender equity,
k) political empowerment, and
l) A balance of power and prosperity of nations.</li>
</ol>
<p>The 2011 World Conference on Social Determinants of Health brought together delegations from 125 member states and resulted in the Rio Political Declaration on Social Determinants of Health.</p>
<p>This declaration involved an affirmation that health inequities are unacceptable, and noted that these inequities arise from the societal conditions in which people are;</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Born,</li>
<li>Grow, live,</li>
<li>Work, and</li>
<li>Age, including</li>
<li>Early childhood development,</li>
<li>Education,</li>
<li>Economic status,</li>
<li>Employment and decent work,</li>
<li>Housing environment, and</li>
<li>Effective prevention and treatment of health problems.</li>
</ol>
<p>The United States Centers for Disease Control defines social determinants of health as "life-enhancing resources:</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Such as food supply,</li>
<li>Housing,</li>
<li>Economic and</li>
<li>Social relationships,</li>
<li>Transportation,</li>
<li>Education, and</li>
<li>Health care, whose distribution across populations effectively determines length and quality of life".</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>These include access to care and resources such as:</p>
<ol>
<li>Food,</li>
<li>Insurance coverage,</li>
<li>Income,</li>
<li>Housing, and</li>
<li>Transportation.</li>
</ol>
<p>Social determinants of health influence health-promoting behaviours, and health equity among the population is not possible without equitable distribution of social determinants among groups.</p>
<p>"The degree to which social conditions affect health is illustrated by the association between education and mortality rates".</p>
<p>Based on the data collected, the social conditions such as education, income, and race were very much dependent on one another, but these social conditions also apply independent health influences.</p>
<p>Marmot and Bell found that in wealthy countries, income and mortality are correlated as a marker of relative position within society, and this relative position is related to social conditions that are important for health including:</p>
<ol>
<li>Good early childhood development,</li>
<li>Access to good quality education,</li>
<li>Rewarding work with some degree of autonomy,</li>
<li>Decent housing, and</li>
<li>A clean and safe living environment.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="concept-and-historical-evolution-of-epidemiology">CONCEPT AND HISTORICAL EVOLUTION OF EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#concept-and-historical-evolution-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>HISTORY OF EPIDEMIOLOGY</strong>: The Greek physician Hippocrates, known as the father of medicine, sought logic to sickness; he is the first person known to have examined the relationships between the occurrence of disease and environmental influences. He coined the terms <em>endemic</em> (for diseases usually found in some places but not in others) and <em>epidemic</em> (for diseases that are seen at some times but not others).</p>
<p>Although epidemiology as a discipline has blossomed since World War II, epidemiologic thinking has been traced from Hippocrates through John Graunt, William Farr, John Snow, and others.</p>
<p>In the mid- and late-1800s, epidemiological methods began to be applied in the investigation of disease occurrence. At that time, most investigators focused on acute infectious diseases. In the 1930s and 1940s, epidemiologists extended their methods to noninfectious diseases. The period since World War II has seen an explosion in the development of research methods and the</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>theoretical underpinnings of epidemiology. Epidemiology has been applied to the entire range of health-related outcomes, behaviors, and even knowledge and attitudes. The studies by Doll and Hill linking lung cancer to smoking and the study of cardiovascular disease among residents of Framingham, Massachusetts are two examples of how pioneering researchers have applied epidemiologic methods to chronic disease since World War II. During the 1960s and early 1970s health workers applied epidemiologic methods to eradicate naturally occurring smallpox worldwide. This was an achievement in applied epidemiology of unprecedented proportions.</p>
<p>In the 1980s, epidemiology was extended to the studies of injuries and violence. In the 1990s, the related fields of molecular and genetic epidemiology (expansion of epidemiology to look at specific pathways, molecules and genes that influence risk of developing disease) took root. Meanwhile, infectious diseases continued to challenge epidemiologists as new infectious agents emerged (Ebola virus, Human Immunodeficiency virus (HIV)/ Acquired Immunodeficiency Syndrome (AIDS)), were identified (Legionella, Severe Acute Respiratory Syndrome (SARS)), or changed (drug-resistant Mycobacterium tuberculosis, Avian influenza). Beginning in the 1990s and accelerating after the terrorist attacks of September 11, 2001, epidemiologists have had to consider not only natural transmission of infectious organisms but also deliberate spread through biologic warfare and bioterrorism.</p>
<p>Today, public health workers throughout the world accept and use epidemiology regularly to characterize the health of their communities and to solve day-to-day problems, large and small.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="uses-of-epidemiology">Uses of Epidemiology:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uses-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Count health-related events</li>
<li>Describe the distribution of health-related events in the population</li>
<li>Describe clinical patterns</li>
<li>Identify risk factors for developing diseases</li>
<li>Identify causes or determinants of disease</li>
<li>Identify control and/or preventive measures</li>
<li>Establish priorities for allocating resources</li>
<li>Select interventions for prevention and control</li>
<li>Evaluate programs</li>
<li>Conduct research on:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>risk factors and causes</li>
<li>drug trials / vaccine trialount health-related events</li>
<li>risk factors and causes</li>
<li>drug trials / vaccine trials</li>
<li>operational research</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="core-epidemiologic-functions">CORE EPIDEMIOLOGIC FUNCTIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#core-epidemiologic-functions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In the mid-1980s, five major tasks of epidemiology in public health practice were identified:</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i. Public health surveillance,
ii. Field investigation, analytic studies,
iii. Evaluation and linkages.</p>
<p>A sixth task, policy development, was recently added. These tasks are described below.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-public-health-surveillance">1. Public health surveillance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-public-health-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Public health surveillance is the ongoing:</p>
<p>i. Systematic collection,
ii. Analysis,
iii. Interpretation, and
iv. Dissemination of health data to help guide public health decision making and action. Surveillance is equivalent to monitoring the pulse of the community.</p>
<p>The purpose of public health surveillance, which is sometimes called “information for action,” is to portray the ongoing patterns of disease occurrence and disease potential so that:</p>
<p>i. Investigation,
ii. Control, and
iii. Prevention measures can be applied efficiently and effectively.</p>
<p>This is accomplished through the systematic collection and evaluation of morbidity and mortality reports and other relevant health information, and the dissemination of these data and their interpretation to those involved in disease control and public health decision making.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="surveillance-cycle">SURVEILLANCE CYCLE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#surveillance-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-4284e1b8.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<p>Morbidity and mortality reports are common sources of surveillance data for local and state health departments. These reports generally are submitted by:</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i. Health-care providers,
ii. Infection control practitioners, or
iii. Laboratories that are required to notify the health department of any patient with a reportable disease such as:
a) Pertussis,
b) Meningococcal meningitis, or
c) AIDS.</p>
<p>Other sources of health-related data that are used for surveillance include:
i. Reports from investigations of individual cases and disease clusters,
ii. Public health program data such as:
a) Immunization coverage in a community,
b) Disease registries and health surveys.</p>
<p>Most often, surveillance relies on simple systems to collect a limited amount of information about each case. Although not every case of disease is reported, health officials regularly review the case reports they do receive and look for patterns among them. These practices have proven invaluable in detecting problems, evaluating programs, and guiding public health action.</p>
<p>While public health surveillance traditionally has focused on communicable diseases, surveillance systems now exist that target:
i. Injuries,
ii. Chronic diseases,
iii. Genetic and birth defects,
iv. Occupational and
v. Potentially environmentally-related diseases, and
vi. Health behaviors.</p>
<p>Since September 11, 2001, a variety of systems that rely on electronic reporting have been developed, including those that report daily emergency department visits, sales of over-the-counter medicines, and worker absenteeism. Because epidemiologists are likely to be called upon to design and use these and other new surveillance systems, an epidemiologist's core competencies must include design of data collection instruments, data management, descriptive methods and graphing, interpretation of data, and scientific writing and presentation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-field-investigation">2. Field investigation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-field-investigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As noted above, surveillance provides information for action. One of the first actions that results from a surveillance case report or report of a cluster is investigation by the public health department. The investigation may be as limited as a phone call to the health-care provider to confirm or clarify the circumstances of the reported case, or it may involve a field investigation</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth
This document is available on
5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>requiring the coordinated efforts of dozens of people to characterize the extent of an epidemic and to identify its cause.</p>
<p>The objectives of such investigations also vary. Investigations often lead to the identification of additional unreported or unrecognized ill persons who might otherwise continue to spread infection to others. For example, one of the hallmarks of investigations of persons with sexually transmitted disease is the identification of sexual partners or contacts of patients. When interviewed, many of these contacts are found to be infected without knowing it, and are given treatment they did not realize they needed. Identification and treatment of these contacts prevents further spread.</p>
<p>For some diseases, investigations may identify a source or vehicle of infection that can be controlled or eliminated. For example, the investigation of a case of <em>Escherichia coli</em> O157:H7 infection usually focuses on trying to identify the vehicle, often ground beef but sometimes something more unusual such as fruit juice. By identifying the vehicle, investigators may be able to determine how many other persons might have already been exposed and how many continue to be at risk. When a commercial product turns out to be the culprit, public announcements and recalling the product may prevent many additional cases.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="symbol-of-eis">Symbol of EIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symbol-of-eis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-f9e45516.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg"></p>
<p>Occasionally, the objective of an investigation may simply be to learn more about:</p>
<p>i. The natural history,
ii. clinical spectrum,
iii. descriptive epidemiology, and
iv. risk factors of the disease before determining what disease intervention methods might be appropriate.</p>
<p>Early investigations of the epidemic of SARS in 2003 were needed to establish a case definition based on the clinical presentation, and to characterize the populations at risk by:</p>
<p>i. Time,
ii. Place, and
iii. Person.</p>
<p>As more was learned about the epidemiology of the disease and communicability of the virus, appropriate recommendations regarding isolation and quarantine were issued.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Field investigations of the type described above are sometimes referred to as “shoe leather epidemiology,” conjuring up images of dedicated, if haggard, epidemiologists beating the pavement in search of additional cases and clues regarding source and mode of transmission. This approach is commemorated in the symbol of the Epidemic Intelligence Service (EIS), CDC's training program for disease detectives — a shoe with a hole in the sole.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-analytic-studies">3. Analytic studies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-analytic-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Surveillance and field investigations are usually sufficient to identify:</p>
<p>i. Causes,
ii. Modes of transmission, and
iii. Appropriate control and prevention measures.</p>
<p>But sometimes analytic studies employing more rigorous methods are needed. Often the methods are used in combination — with surveillance and field investigations providing clues or hypotheses about causes and modes of transmission, and analytic studies evaluating the credibility of those hypotheses.</p>
<p>Clusters or outbreaks of disease frequently are investigated initially with descriptive epidemiology. The descriptive approach involves the study of disease incidence and distribution by:</p>
<p>i. Time,
ii. Place, and
iii. Person.</p>
<p>It includes the calculation of rates and identification of parts of the population at higher risk than others. Occasionally, when the association between exposure and disease is quite strong, the investigation may stop when descriptive epidemiology is complete and control measures may be implemented immediately. More frequently, descriptive studies, like case investigations, generate hypotheses that can be tested with analytic studies. While some field investigations are conducted in response to acute health problems such as outbreaks, many others are planned studies.</p>
<p>The hallmark of an analytic epidemiologic study is the use of a valid comparison group. Epidemiologists must be skilled in all aspects of such studies, including:</p>
<ol>
<li>design,</li>
<li>conduct,</li>
<li>analysis,</li>
<li>interpretation, and</li>
<li>Communication of findings.</li>
</ol>
<p>a. Design includes:</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i. Determining the appropriate research strategy and study design,
ii. Writing justifications and protocols,
iii. Calculating sample sizes,
iv. Deciding on criteria for subject selection (e.g., developing case definitions),
v. Choosing an appropriate comparison group, and
vi. Designing questionnaires.</p>
<p>b. Conduct involves:
i. Securing appropriate clearances and approvals,
ii. Adhering to appropriate ethical principles,
iii. Abstracting records,
iv. Tracking down and interviewing subjects,
v. Collecting and handling specimens, and
vi. Managing the data.</p>
<p>c. Analysis begins with describing the characteristics of the subjects. It progresses to:
i. Calculation of rates,
ii. Creation of comparative tables (e.g., two-by-two tables), and
iii. computation of measures of association (e.g., risk ratios or odds ratios), tests of significance (e.g., chi-square test),
iv. Confidence intervals and the like.</p>
<p>Many epidemiologic studies require more advanced analytic techniques such as:
i. Stratified analysis,
ii. Regression, and
iii. Modeling.</p>
<p>d. Finally, interpretation involves:
i. Putting the study findings into perspective,
ii. Identifying the key take-home messages, and
iii. Making sound recommendations.</p>
<p>Doing so requires that the epidemiologist be knowledgeable about the subject matter and the strengths and weaknesses of the study.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Evaluation: Epidemiologists, who are accustomed to using systematic and quantitative approaches, have come to play an important role in evaluation of public health services and other activities. Evaluation is the process of determining, as systematically and objectively as possible:</li>
</ol>
<p>c) The relevance,</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>d) Effectiveness,
e) Efficiency, and
f) Impact of activities with respect to established goals.</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>
<p>Effectiveness refers to the ability of a program to produce the intended or expected results in the field; effectiveness differs from efficacy, which is the ability to produce results under ideal conditions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Efficiency refers to the ability of the program to produce the intended results with a minimum expenditure of time and resources.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The evaluation itself may focus on:</p>
<p>i. Plans (formative evaluation),
ii. Operations (process evaluation),
iii. Impact (summative evaluation), or
iv. Outcomes — or any combination of these.</p>
<p>Evaluation of an immunization program, for example, might assess the efficiency of the operations, the proportion of the target population immunized, and the apparent impact of the program on the incidence of vaccine-preventable diseases. Similarly, evaluation of a surveillance system might address operations and attributes of the system, its ability to detect cases or outbreaks, and its usefulness.</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>Linkages: Epidemiologists working in public health settings rarely act in isolation. In fact, field epidemiology is often said to be a "team sport." During an investigation an epidemiologist usually participates as either a member or the leader of a multidisciplinary team. Other team members may be:</li>
</ol>
<p>i. Laboratorians,
ii. Sanitarians,
iii. Infection control personnel,
iv. Nurses or other
v. Clinical staff, and, increasingly,
vi. Computer information specialists.</p>
<p>Many outbreaks cross geographical and jurisdictional lines, so co-investigators may be from local, state, or federal levels of government, academic institutions, clinical facilities, or the private sector. To promote current and future collaboration, the epidemiologists need to maintain relationships with staff of other agencies and institutions. Mechanisms for sustaining such linkages include official memoranda of understanding, sharing of</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>published or on-line information for public health audiences and outside partners, and informal networking that takes place at professional meetings.</p>
<ol start="8">
<li><strong>Policy development</strong>: The definition of epidemiology ends with the following phrase: “...and the application of this study to the control of health problems.” While some academically minded epidemiologists have stated that epidemiologists should stick to research and not get involved in policy developments or even make recommendations. Public health epidemiologists do not have this luxury. Indeed epidemiologist who understand a problem and the population in which it occurs are often in a uniquely qualified position to recommend appropriate interventions. As a result, epidemiologists working in public health regularly provide input, testimony and recommendations regarding disease control strategies, reportable disease regulations, and health - care policy.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiological-approaches">Epidemiological Approaches<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiological-approaches" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As with all scientific endeavors, the practice of epidemiology relies on a systematic approach.</p>
<p>In very simple terms Epidemiologist:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p><strong>Counts</strong>:
i. cases or
ii. health events, and
iii. describes them in terms of:
a) time,
b) place, and
c) person;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Divides</strong> the number of cases by appropriate denominator to calculate rates; and</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Compares</strong> these rates over time or for different groups of people.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Before counting cases, however, the epidemiologist must decide what a case is.</p>
<p>This is done by developing a case definition.</p>
<p>Then, using this case definition, the epidemiologist finds and collects information about the case-patients.</p>
<p>The epidemiologist then performs descriptive epidemiology by characterizing the cases collectively according to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Time,</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Place, and</li>
<li>Person.</li>
</ol>
<p>To calculate the disease rate, the epidemiologist divides the number of cases by the size of the population.</p>
<p>Finally, to determine whether this rate is greater than what one would normally expect, and if so identify factors contributing to this increase, the epidemiologist compares the rate from this population to the rate in an appropriate comparison group, using analytic epidemiology techniques.</p>
<p>Before counting cases, the epidemiologist must decide what to count, that is, what to call a case.</p>
<p>For that, the epidemiologist uses a case definition.</p>
<p>A case definition is a set standard criterion for classifying whether a person has a particular:</p>
<ol>
<li>Disease,</li>
<li>Syndrome, or</li>
<li>Other health condition.</li>
</ol>
<p>Populations are used for Epidemiological studies, but more specific characteristics and outcomes at the individual level.</p>
<p>The following approaches may also be used:</p>
<ol>
<li>Routinely available data e.g. hospital out-patient, birth and deaths, registration of persons data e.t.c.</li>
<li>Disease surveillance: close observation of circumstances that are likely to cause disease.</li>
<li>Health systems research or approach [approach to health delivery] which includes:</li>
</ol>
<p>i. Equitable access to health service
ii. The quality and responsiveness of health services,
iii. The efficiency and effectiveness of interventions,
iv. A combination of the three elements above together determine the out-come of service delivery.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>51</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>Community studies [community diagnosis].</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>KAPB [knowledge, attitude, practice and belief] surveys.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Qualitative Epidemiology, involving distinctions based on qualities or relating to or involving comparison based on qualities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Epidemiological studies like:
i. Case control studies: used to study associations between environmental or genetic factors suspected from ecologic associations.
ii. Cohort [group] studies: used for the study of a more intensive resource study.
iii. Meta-analysis: used to analyze data accumulated from several separate studies.
iv. Human experimentation: used to test effects on a controlled population.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Epidemiological approaches can be broadly classified as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>A diagnostic phase, in which the presence of the disease is confirmed.</li>
<li>A descriptive phase, which describes the population at risk and the distribution of disease, both in time and space, within these populations. This may then allow a series of hypotheses to be formed about the likely determinants of the disease and the effects of these on the frequency with which the disease occurs in populations at risk.</li>
</ol>
<p>This phase captures disease distribution and frequency.</p>
<p>It includes questions like:
a) What is the problem?
b) What is the frequency?
c) Who is involved?</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>d) Where does it occur?
e) When does it occur?</p>
<p>Answers to the above questions provide clues to the factors which determine the occurrence of diseases/health problems.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>An investigating phase, which normally involves the implementation of a series of field studies designed to test these hypotheses.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>An Experimental phase, in which experiments are performed under controlled conditions to test these hypotheses in more detail, should the results of phase three prove promising. This phase is normally applied in clinical and community trials to answer questions about the effectiveness of new methods for controlling diseases or for improving underlying conditions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Analytical phase, in which the results produced by the above investigations are analyzed. This is often combined with attempts to model epidemiology of the disease using information generated. Such a process often enables the epidemiologist to determine whether any vital bits of information about the disease process are missing. It attempts to analyze causes or determinants of diseases by testing hypothesis to answer questions such as:</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>a) How is the disease caused and
b) Why is it continuing?</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>An intervention phase: aim at seeing the effects of active intervention which results in either the exposure or the prevention of exposure of a group of people to some determinant or risk factor.</li>
</ol>
<p>In this approach, appropriate methods of control of the disease are examined either under experimental conditions or in the field to answer questions about the effectiveness of new methods for controlling diseases or for improving underlying conditions. Interventions in the disease process are affected by manipulating existing determinants or introducing new ones.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>53</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>
<p>A decision-making phase, in which knowledge of the epidemiology of the disease is used to explore the various options available for its control. This often involves the modeling of the effects that these different options are likely to have on the incidence of the disease. These models can be combined with other models that examine the costs of the various control measures and compare them with the benefits, in terms of increased productivity, that these measures are likely to produce. The optimum control strategy can then be selected as a result of the expected decrease in disease incidence in the populations at risk.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A monitoring phase, which takes place during the implementation of the control measures are to ensure that these measures are being properly applied, are having desired effect on reducing disease incidence, and that developments that are likely to jeopardize the success of the control program are quickly detected.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Evaluative phase: in this phase, attempts are made to measure the effectiveness of different health services.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="descriptive-epidemiology">DESCRIPTIVE EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#descriptive-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Descriptive epidemiology seeks to summarize conditions based on:</p>
<ol>
<li>person,</li>
<li>place, and</li>
<li>Time by analyzing disease (or other outcomes of exposure) patterns.</li>
</ol>
<p>Through data collection, this field seeks to understand a population’s health status, make hypotheses about the causes of diseases, and inform program planning and evaluation. In other words, descriptive epidemiology describes the distribution of disease (recall that epidemiology is the study of the distribution and determinants of disease in populations). This is done through describing a condition by various characteristics of person (race, age, or sex, for example), place (geographic location), and time (a specific year or a span of time).</p>
<p>In order to make inferences about causes or determinants of health conditions—the second part of the definition of epidemiology—one must conduct studies to study the relationship between exposures (risk or protective factors) and outcomes.</p>
<p>There are two major types of studies:</p>
<ol>
<li>Experimental studies and</li>
<li>Observational studies.</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>In public health and epidemiology, most studies are observational in nature. Although an outcome may be a disease, we will primarily refer to outcomes as any result of interest that may be linked to a specific exposure (which may be a risk factor, protective factor, or intervention).</p>
<p><strong>Study Designs:</strong> There are two main types of study designs:</p>
<p>i. Descriptive studies [designed to collect facts].
ii. Analytical studies [designed to test hypothesis and examine cause-effect-relationship.</p>
<p><strong>Descriptive Study Design:</strong> This study is limited to description of occurrence of disease in a population and is often the first step in epidemiological investigation.</p>
<p>Descriptive studies make no attempt to analyze the link between exposures and effect. They are designed to describe certain characteristics of a problem or variables as WHO, WHERE, and WHEN [person, place and time] by collecting information on specific diseases in specified population groups.</p>
<p><strong>Who - in terms of:</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Sex,</li>
<li>Age,</li>
<li>Occupation,</li>
<li>Income,</li>
<li>Culture,</li>
<li>Religion, and</li>
<li>Nutritional status.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Where</strong> – the place where people live or work may determine which disease and/or the health problems they suffer from.</p>
<p><strong>When</strong> – it is important to know when the health problem is severe or the incidence of new cases.</p>
<p><strong>Descriptive studies may be:</strong></p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Cross-sectional study</strong>, which investigates individual at single point in time thus giving estimates of point prevalence.</li>
<li><strong>Longitudinal study</strong>, which measures individuals over a period of time, thus giving estimates of incidences. Longitudinal studies may either be:</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>55</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i. Prospective [looking forward], measuring individuals as they become ill or recover, or
ii. Retrospective [looking back], studying events of illness that occurred in the study population in the past.</p>
<p>The information which is obtained may enable us to develop hypothesis about the pattern of disease.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="analytical-epidemiology">ANALYTICAL EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#analytical-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Analytical Epidemiology is a study designed to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Investigate hypothesized causal relationships.</li>
<li>Tries to determine why disease is occurring.</li>
<li>Tests hypotheses.</li>
</ol>
<p>Analytical Study Design - This attempts to analyze causes or determinants of disease by testing hypotheses [A proposal intended to explain certain facts or observations] to answer such questions as how is disease caused, its significance, and important causative or predisposing factors so as to take appropriate action for solution.</p>
<p>Analytical study design also tests hypotheses about the influence which determines that one person is affected by disease and another one is not.</p>
<p>Analytical studies are designed to explain the distribution of diseases in specified population groups by testing hypotheses.</p>
<p>These hypotheses may be arrived at from a:</p>
<ol>
<li>Descriptive study,</li>
<li>Clinical observation or from</li>
<li>An examination of existing records.</li>
</ol>
<p>Analytical studies attempt to show whether particular events e.g. consumption of certain foods or states such as malnutrition or living in overcrowded conditions acts as "causes "whose "effects" is the resulting disease.</p>
<p>Analytical studies are therefore based on the comparison of two or more groups of individuals. Three kinds of comparisons can be made and there are correspondingly three types of analytical studies namely:</p>
<ol>
<li>Cross-sectional study,</li>
<li>Retrospective study,</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Cohort [A group of people having approximately the same age] or prospective study.</li>
</ol>
<p>The effects of the intervention on the study group are determined by comparing this group with a similar group of controls who did not receive the intervention. The evaluation of a disease control program is a type of intervention study.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="natural-history-of-disease">NATURAL HISTORY OF DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#natural-history-of-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The natural history of disease is the course a disease takes in individual people from its pathological onset ("inception") until its eventual resolution through complete recovery or death. The inception of a disease is not a firmly defined concept. The natural history of a disease is sometimes said to start at the moment of exposure to causal agents. Knowledge of the natural history of disease ranks alongside causal understanding in importance for disease prevention and control. Natural history of disease is one of the major elements of descriptive epidemiology.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chain-of-infection">CHAIN OF INFECTION,<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chain-of-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-eca8bad0.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<p>For an infection to develop, each link of the chain must be connected. Breaking any link of the chain can stop the transmission of infection.</p>
<p>INFECTIOUS DISEASE: Any microorganism that can cause a disease such as a bacterium, virus, parasite, or fungus.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>57</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Reasons that the organism will cause an infection are virulence (ability to multiply and grow), invasiveness (ability to enter tissue), and pathogenicity (ability to cause disease).</p>
<p><strong>RESERVOIR</strong>: The place where the microorganism resides, thrives, and reproduces, i.e., food, water, toilet seat, elevator buttons, human feces, respiratory secretions.</p>
<p><strong>PORTAL OF EXIT</strong>: The place where the organism leaves the reservoir, such as the respiratory tract (nose, mouth), intestinal tract (rectum), urinary tract, orblood and other body fluids.</p>
<p><strong>MODE OF TRANSMISSION</strong>: The means by which an organism transfers from one carrier to another by either direct transmission (direct contact between infectious host and susceptible host) or indirect transmission (which involves an intermediate carrier like an environmental surface or piece of medical equipment).</p>
<p><strong>PORTAL OF ENTRY</strong>: The opening where an infectious disease enters the host’s body such as mucus membranes, open wounds, or tubes inserted in body cavities like urinary catheters or feeding tubes.</p>
<p><strong>SUSCEPTIBLE HOST</strong>: The person who is at risk for developing an infection from the disease. Several factors make a person more susceptible to disease including age (young people and elderly people generally are more at risk), underlying chronic diseases such as diabetes or asthma, conditions that weaken the immune system like HIV, certain types of medications, invasive devices like feeding tubes, and malnutrition. For an infection to develop, each link of the chain must be connected. Breaking any link of the chain can stop the transmission of infection!</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemic-disease-occurrence">EPIDEMIC DISEASE OCCURRENCE.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemic-disease-occurrence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An epidemic is the rapid spread of infectious disease to a large number of people in a given population within a short period of time, usually two weeks or less.</p>
<p>Epidemics of infectious disease are generally caused by several factors including:</p>
<ol>
<li>A change in the ecology of the host population (e.g. increased stress or increase in the density of a vector species),</li>
<li>A genetic change in the pathogen reservoir or the introduction of an emerging pathogen to a host population (by movement of pathogen or host).</li>
</ol>
<p>Generally, an epidemic occurs when host immunity to either an established pathogen or newly emerging novel pathogen is suddenly reduced below that found in the endemic equilibrium and the transmission threshold is exceeded.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>An epidemic may be restricted to one location; however, if it spreads to other countries or continents and affects a substantial number of people, it may be termed a pandemic.</p>
<p><strong>Causes:</strong> There are several changes that may occur in an infectious agent that may trigger an epidemic. These include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Increased virulence</li>
<li>Introduction into a novel setting</li>
<li>Changes in host susceptibility to the infectious agent</li>
</ol>
<p>The conditions which govern the outbreak of epidemics include infected food supplies such as contaminated drinking water and the migration of populations of certain animals, such as rats or mosquitoes, which can act as disease vectors. Certain epidemics occur at certain seasons: for example, whooping-cough occurs in spring, whereas measles produces two epidemics, one in winter and one in March. Influenza, the common cold, and other infections of the upper respiratory tract, such as sore throat, occur predominantly in the winter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-out-breaks">TYPES OF OUT BREAKS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-out-breaks" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p><strong>Common source outbreak:</strong> In a common source outbreak, the affected individuals had an exposure to a common agent. If the exposure is singular and all of the affected individuals develop the disease over a single exposure and incubation course, it can be termed a point source outbreak. If the exposure was continuous or variable, it can be termed a continuous outbreak or intermittent outbreak, respectively.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Propagated outbreak:</strong> In a propagated outbreak, the disease spreads from person-to-person. Affected individuals may become independent reservoirs leading to further exposures.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Many epidemics will have characteristics of both common source and propagated outbreaks. For example, secondary person-to-person spread may occur after a common source exposure or environmental vectors may spread a zoonotic diseases agent.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="transmission">Transmission:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Airborne transmission: Airborne transmission is the spread of infection by droplet nuclei or dust in the air. Without the intervention of winds or drafts the distance over which airborne infection takes place is short, say 10 to 20 feet.</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>59</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Arthropod transmission: Arthropod transmission takes place by an insect, either mechanically through a contaminated proboscis or feet, or biologically when there is growth or replication of an organism in the arthropod.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Biological transmission: Involving a biological process, e.g. passing a stage of development of the infecting agent in an intermediate host. Opposite to mechanical transmission.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Colostral transmission: A form of vertical transmission via successive generations.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Contact transmission: The disease agent is transferred directly by biting, sucking, chewing or indirectly by inhalation of droplets, drinking of contaminated water, traveling in contaminated vehicles.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cyclopropagative transmission: The agent undergoes both development and multiplication in the transmitting vehicle.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Developmental transmission: The agent undergoes some development in the transmission vehicle.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Fecal-oral transmission: The infectious agent is shed by the infected host in feces and acquired by the susceptible host through ingestion of contaminated material.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Horizontal transmission: Lateral spread to others in the same group and at the same time; spread to contemporaries.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Mechanical transmission: The transmitter is not infected in that tissues are not invaded and the agent does not multiply.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Preparative transmission: The agent multiplies in the transmission vehicle.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="12">
<li>Vertical transmission: From one generation to the next, perhaps transovarially or by intrauterine infection of the fetus. Some retroviruses are transmitted in the germ line, i.e. their genetic material is integrated into the DNA of either the ovum or sperm.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disease-prevention">DISEASE PREVENTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disease-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Preventive healthcare (alternately preventive medicine or prophylaxis) consists of measures taken for disease prevention, as opposed to disease treatment.</p>
<p>Just as health encompasses a variety of physical and mental states, so do disease and disability, which are affected by:</p>
<ol>
<li>Environmental factors,</li>
<li>Genetic predisposition, disease agents, and</li>
<li>Lifestyle choices.</li>
</ol>
<p>Health, disease, and disability are dynamic processes which begin before individuals realize they are affected. Disease prevention relies on anticipatory actions that can be categorized as:</p>
<ol>
<li>Primary,</li>
<li>Secondary, and</li>
<li>Tertiary prevention.</li>
</ol>
<p>Each year, millions of people die preventable deaths.</p>
<p>Preventive healthcare is especially important given the worldwide rise in prevalence of chronic diseases and deaths from these diseases.</p>
<p>There are many methods for prevention of disease.</p>
<p>Some common disease screenings include checking for:</p>
<ol>
<li>hypertension (high blood pressure),</li>
<li>hyperglycemia (high blood sugar, a risk factor for diabetes mellitus),</li>
<li>hypercholesterolemia (high blood cholesterol),</li>
<li>screening for colon cancer,</li>
<li>depression,</li>
<li>HIV and</li>
<li>other common types of sexually transmitted disease such as:
i. Chlamydia,
ii. syphilis, and
iii. gonorrhea,
iv. mammography (to screen for breast cancer),</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>61</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>v. colorectal cancer screening,
vi. a pap test (to check for cervical cancer), and
vii. Screening for osteoporosis.</p>
<p>Genetic testing can also be performed to screen for mutations that cause genetic disorders or predisposition to certain diseases such as breast or ovarian cancer.</p>
<p>However, these measures are not affordable for every individual and the cost effectiveness of preventive healthcare is still a topic of debate.</p>
<p><strong>Levels of Disease prevention:</strong> Preventive healthcare strategies are typically described as taking place at the:</p>
<ol>
<li>Primary,</li>
<li>Secondary, and</li>
</ol>
<p>These levels might be better described as:</p>
<ol>
<li>"Prevention,</li>
<li>Treatment, and</li>
<li>Rehabilitation though the terms:</li>
</ol>
<p>i. Primary,
ii. Secondary, and
iii. Tertiary prevention are still commonly in use today.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Level</th><th>Definition</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Primary prevention</td><td>Methods to avoid occurrence of disease either through eliminating disease agents or increasing resistance to disease.</td></tr><tr><td>Examples include immunization against disease, maintaining a healthy diet and exercise regimen, and avoiding smoking.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Secondary prevention</td><td>Methods to detect and address an existing disease prior to the appearance of symptoms. Examples include treatment of hypertension (a risk factor for many cardiovascular diseases), cancer screenings.</td></tr><tr><td>Tertiary prevention</td><td>Methods to reduce negative impact of symptomatic disease, such as disability or death, through rehabilitation and treatment.</td></tr><tr><td>Examples include surgical procedures that halt the spread or progression of disease.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Primary prevention: Primary prevention consists of "health promotion" and "specific protection.</p>
<p>Health promotion activities are non-clinical life choices, for example, eating nutritious meals and exercising daily, that both prevents disease and creates a sense of overall well-being.</p>
<p>Preventing disease and creating overall well-being, prolongs our life expectancy.</p>
<p>Health-promotional activities do not target a specific disease or condition but rather promote health and well-being on a very general level.</p>
<p>On the other hand, specific protection targets a type or group of diseases and complements the goals of health promotion.</p>
<p>In the case of a sexually transmitted disease such as syphilis health promotion activities would include avoiding microorganisms by maintaining personal hygiene, routine check-up appointments with the doctor, general sex education, etc. whereas specific protective measures would be using prophylactics (such as condoms) during sex and avoiding sexual promiscuity.</p>
<p>Food is very much the most basic tool in preventive health care.</p>
<p>Secondary prevention: Secondary prevention deals with latent diseases and attempts to prevent an asymptomatic disease from progressing to symptomatic disease.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>63</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Certain diseases can be classified as primary or secondary.</p>
<p>This depends on definitions of what constitutes a disease, though, in general, primary prevention addresses the root cause of a disease or injury whereas secondary prevention aims to detect and treat a disease early on.</p>
<p>Secondary prevention consists of "early diagnosis and prompt treatment" to contain the disease and prevent its spread to other individuals, and "disability limitation" to prevent potential future complications and disabilities from the disease.</p>
<p>Disability limitation for syphilitic patients includes continued check-ups on the:</p>
<ol>
<li>Heart,</li>
<li>Cerebrospinal fluid, and</li>
<li>Central nervous system of patients to curb any damaging effects such as blindness or paralysis.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Tertiary prevention</strong>: Finally, tertiary prevention attempts to reduce the damage caused by symptomatic disease by focusing on:</p>
<ol>
<li>Mental, physical, and</li>
<li>Social rehabilitation.</li>
</ol>
<p>Unlike secondary prevention, which aims to prevent disability, the objective of tertiary prevention is to maximize the remaining capabilities and functions of an already disabled patient. Goals of tertiary prevention include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Preventing pain and</li>
<li>Damage,</li>
<li>Halting progression and</li>
<li>Complications from disease, and</li>
<li>Restoring the health and functions of the individuals affected by disease.</li>
</ol>
<p>The three levels of prevention each have corresponding health programs:</p>
<p>As for the primary level of intervention, Primary preventive measures would include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Educating,</li>
<li>Counseling,</li>
<li>Lifestyle modifications,</li>
<li>Health education programs for communities,</li>
<li>Wellness and fitness programs,</li>
<li>Mandatory immunization for children,</li>
<li>Environmental sanitation activities,</li>
<li>The adoption other specific health practices and other programs geared towards the safety and wellness of the community.</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Primordial Prevention: Primordial prevention is defined as prevention of risk factors themselves, beginning with change in social and environmental conditions in which these factors are observed to develop, and continuing for high risk children, adolescents and young adults.</p>
<p>Disease Prevention Strategy: The strategy provides evidence-based recommendations that are fundamental to improving the nation's health through the active engagement of all sectors of society to help achieve four broad strategic directions:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Building Healthy and Safe Community Environments: Prevention of disease starts in our communities and at home; not just in the doctor's office. For example, businesses and employers can adopt practices to encourage their workforce to increase physical activity and reduce pollution (e.g., workplace flexibility, rideshare and vanpool programs, park-and-ride incentives, travel demand management initiatives, and telecommuting options).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Expanding Quality Preventive Services in Both Clinical and Community Settings: When people receive preventive care, such as immunizations and cancer screenings, they have better health and lower health care costs. For example, expanding the linkages between clinical and community prevention efforts, such as diabetes prevention programs that support preventive efforts among underserved groups and can improve access to preventive services.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Empowering People to Make Healthy Choices: Policies and programs can make healthy options the easy and affordable choice, and when people have access to actionable and easy-to-understand information and resources, they are empowered to make healthier choices. For example, health care professionals can use multiple communication tools (e.g., mobile phone applications, personal health records, and credible health websites) and culturally competent methods to support more traditional written and oral communication.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Eliminating Health Disparities: By eliminating disparities in achieving and maintaining health, we can help improve quality of life for all Americans. For example, health care providers can train and hire more qualified staff from underrepresented racial and ethnic minority groups and people with disabilities.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>To help achieve these goals, the Strategy identifies evidence-based recommendations that are most likely to reduce the burden of leading causes of preventable death and major illness.</p>
<p>The Strategy's seven priority areas are:</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
