<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol>
<li>Tobacco free living</li>
<li>Preventing drug abuse and excessive alcohol use</li>
<li>Healthy eating</li>
<li>Active living</li>
<li>Injury and violence-free living</li>
<li>Reproductive and sexual health</li>
<li>Mental and emotional wellbeing</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="measures-of-epidemiology">MEASURES OF EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measures-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are two measures used in Epidemiology namely:</p>
<ol>
<li>Prevalence rate.</li>
<li>Incidence rate.</li>
</ol>
<p>i. Incidence: counts new cases of the disease (or outcome)
ii. Prevalence: counts new and existing cases of the disease (or outcome)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="incidence-new-cases">INCIDENCE (NEW CASES)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incidence-new-cases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Incidence quantifies development of disease. Incidence can be estimated using data from a disease registry data or a cohort trial. There is an implicit assumption of a period of time, such as new cases within a month (or a year).</p>
<p>A summary incidence rate can estimate risk (e.g., probability of disease in an individual) if risk is constant across the summarized groups.</p>
<p>As defined, incidence is a count of new cases. However it is often expressed as a proportion of those at risk. The denominator includes all persons at-risk for the disease or condition, i.e. disease-free or condition-free individuals in the population at the start of the time period. Persons in the denominator, those at-risk, should be able to appear in the numerator. Obviously, the denominator would not include persons who already have the disease or condition. Incidence can also be expressed in terms of person-time at risk.</p>
<p>Rates are usually expressed per 100, 1,000, or 100,000 persons. In a strict application, "rate" should only be used when the denominator is an estimate of the total person-time at risk. (You will find the term "rate" used inconsistently in epidemiologic reports. It is better to seek the source of the numbers than to rely on the nomenclature [A system of words used to name things in a particular discipline].)</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="two-common-measures-of-incidence">Two Common Measures of Incidence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#two-common-measures-of-incidence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The cumulative incidence rate (also known as incidence rate, especially for public health surveillance) consists of the number of persons who newly experience the disease or studied outcome during a specified period of time divided by the (average) total population at risk. This calculation assumes all persons in denominator contribute an equal amount of time to the measure. It is usually presented with an upper and lower bound.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="incidence-rates">Incidence Rates<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incidence-rates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Incidence is the relative frequency of occurrence of something.</p>
<p>Incidence rates are the measure of the risk of developing some new condition within a specified time. Although sometimes loosely expressed as the number of new cases during sometime period, it is better expressed as a proportion or rate with a denominator.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="incidence">Incidence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incidence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>proportion is the number of new cases within a specified period divided by the size of the population initially at risk. For example, if a population initially contains 1000 non diseased persons and 28 diseased or cases over two years of observation, the incidence proportion is 28 cases per 1,000 persons i.e. 28%</p>
<p>Incidence rates are designed to provide measures of rate at which people without a disease, illness, and injury develop the conditions during a specified period of time eg a number of new cases in a population over a period of time stated as the number of case which came into existence within a certain period of time per specified unit of population.</p>
<p>In actual application of incidence rates, it is challenging as times of onset of disease may be unclear. In such cases,</p>
<ol>
<li>Time of diagnosis</li>
<li>Date of reporting</li>
<li>Time of appearance of symptoms</li>
<li>Visit to hospital</li>
<li>Appearance at the emergency room or</li>
<li>At other times element of factor that identify the beginning of an outbreak may be used.</li>
</ol>
<p>Incidence is not the onset of an outbreak, but the frequency of new cases or events in the time period and in a specific population.</p>
<p>A calculation of incidence rate involves:</p>
<p>a) The number of cases.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>b) Total population.
c) Period of time.</p>
<p>Incidence rate:
= Number of new cases of a disease occurring in the population during a specified Period of time x 1000</p>
<p>Number of persons exposed to risk of developing the disease during that period of time
Or</p>
<p>Incidence rate:
= Number of a disease or new cases over a period of time x 1,000</p>
<p>Population at risk of developing the disease.</p>
<p>For example, if there were 100 cases of TB in the catchment area of 20,000 [total population] in 12 month [period of time].</p>
<p>Calculate the incidence.</p>
<p>Incidence = New cases x 1,000</p>
<p>Total population
= 100 x 1,000
20,000</p>
<p>Incidence therefore = 5 cases per 1,000 population per year.</p>
<p>This means that in 12 months period you can expect to see 5 cases of TB for every 1,000 in your catchment area.</p>
<p>Incident rates may be used to measure short time acute communicable diseases such as cholera or measles or may be used to measure chronic or long time conditions such as cancer or diabetes mellitus.</p>

Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Incidence Rates Denominator/Numerator issues: The denominator as used in the incidence rates should accurately set forth the number at risk or under study in the group or population.</p>
<p>N/B as the time passes, the number of persons at risk in the population changes. Some people are exposed to the risk at the end of the period while other people are removed from risk shortly after the defined period starts.</p>
<p>To accommodate the transient population problem, the epidemiologist picks the population number/cases at midpoint in the time period to represent the average population at risk.</p>
<p>Since incidence rate is used to study the new cases of a disease, only those individuals at risk of developing the disease i.e. population at risk should be individuals in the denominator. The denominator should include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Individuals with the disease.</li>
<li>Those who have already had the disease [if they no longer susceptible].</li>
<li>Those not susceptible due to immunization.</li>
</ol>
<p>Numerator in Incidence: The numerator in incidence provides specific information about the occurrences of a disease. If the number of cases or persons can vary, the numerator should clearly reflect the differences. The numerator is the number of new cases or incidences starting within time period.</p>
<p>There are two approaches of incidence rates that are used:</p>
<ol>
<li>Cumulative incidence rate: This is the number of person's at risk and is used as denominator. In case of this nature, cumulative incidence rates useful longitudinal studies due time and secular variations. The cumulative incidence is used to study a group of persons followed over a period of time.</li>
<li>Incidence density or Force morbidity: This is the number of cases of disease which occur per unit of a population and at a point in unit time. It is used to predict the risk that a well person has of getting ill over a certain period of time. The population and time element are used in the denominator; whereas the numerator includes new cases of disease or disorder.</li>
</ol>
<p>Force morbidity is also referred to as incidence density.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>

<p>69</p>

<p>Note: the prevalence rate equals the incidence rate multiplied by the average duration of the disease.</p>
<p>For example, if the average duration of a disease is three years and its incidence rate is 10 per 1000; the prevalence rate would be 30 per 1000.</p>
<p>The duration of disease is usually measured from the time of diagnosis to death.</p>
<p>In summary, the incidence rate is an accurate estimate of the risk probability of getting a disease within a certain time period and the denominator is only “at risk” population.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-of-incidence-rate">Principles of Incidence Rate:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-of-incidence-rate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>An incidence rate can be used to estimate the probability of or risk of developing a disease during a specific period.</li>
<li>As the incidence rate goes up, the risk possibility or the probability of getting the disease goes up.</li>
<li>Time- if the incidence rate is consistently higher during a specified time of the year, such as rainy season, the risk of developing diseases goes up at that time eg cholera or malaria are highest during rainy seasons.</li>
<li>Place- if the incidence rates are consistently higher, among people, who live in a certain place, the risk goes up for developing disease if one lives within the area.</li>
<li>Person- if incidence rates are consistently higher among individuals with specific life style factor the risk goes up among that group eg lung cancer increases among cigarette smokers.</li>
<li>Higher incidence implies many new cases, thus risk increases.</li>
<li>When incidence of disease is known to be high, the existence of or potential for an epidemic becomes known and predictable.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reflections">Reflections:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reflections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important to note that incidence is a bit like a motion picture of the development of a disease in a population. It provides a picture of the measure of the movement of disease through a population, the rate at which people without disease develop the disease during a specific period of time.</p>
<p>i. Incidence is a fundamental tool for studying the etiologic factors of both acute and chronic illness.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>ii. Incidence rates are important because they provide a direct measure of the rate at which individuals in a given population become ill and thus provide basis for statements about probability or risk of illness.</p>
<p>Prevalence varies in direct relation to both duration and incidence.</p>
<p>Successful intervention and treatment will prolong life and will have an effect on reducing prevalence. The decrease in incidence and shortening of the duration of a disease decreases the prevalence</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevalence-new-and-old-cases">PREVALENCE (NEW AND OLD CASES)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevalence-new-and-old-cases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Since prevalence counts both new and existing cases, the duration of the disease affects the prevalence. Diseases with a long duration will be more prevalent than those with shorter duration. Chronic, non-fatal conditions are more prevalent than conditions with high mortality. The prevalence of disease is directly related to the duration of the disease:</p>
<p>Prevalence = Incidence × Average Duration</p>
<p>Prevalence is not an apt descriptor of an acute condition.</p>
<p>Similar to incidence, persons included in the denominator must have the potential for being in the numerator, i.e. at-risk for the disease or condition. Prevalence is often expressed after multiplication by 100 (%), 1000 or 100,000.</p>
<p>The term rate may be inappropriately applied to prevalence. Prevalence is a proportion, usually reflecting the proportion with a disease at a particular time. (Point prevalence)</p>
<p>The prevalence pool is the subset of the population with the condition of interest. The prevalence pool is not generally useful for hypothesis-driven epidemiologic research because these are not new cases, but can be useful in tracking the natural history of the disease, evaluating effects of treatments or disease burden.</p>
<p>For most etiologic research, incidence is the more appropriate measure. Studying the incidence of a rare condition however, poses a challenge. Given a small number of new cases, it can be preferable to estimate prevalence instead of incidence in these situations. For example, birth defect rates, reported as the numbers of cases/live births is a prevalent measure. Similarly, an autopsy rate is a prevalent measure.</p>
<p>Two common measures are point prevalence and period prevalence. The difference is whether the estimate is made over a period of time or at one specific time as illustrated below:</p>
<p>Point prevalence: prevalence of condition of interest at a specific time.</p>
<p>Number of existing cases on a specific date/ Number in the defined population on this date</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>0</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Point prevalence ranges from 0 to 100. (%)</p>
<p>Point prevalence can be estimated from a cross-sectional survey or disease registry data by calculating the percentage with a particular disease or condition on a particular date. E.g. what percentage had a particular type of flu on 1/17/2009?</p>
<p><strong>Period prevalence</strong>: prevalence of outcome of interest during a specified period of time. Less frequently used.</p>
<p>Number of cases that occurred in a specified period of time/ Number in the defined population during this period</p>
<p>Period prevalence generally ranges from 0 to 100%. (Theoretically, period prevalence can exceed 100% if you allow individuals who had the disease more than once to be counted for each case of the disease within the reporting period.)</p>
<p>E.g. what percentage of the population had an episode of flu between October and May in the most recent flu season?</p>
<p><strong>Prevalence Rate</strong>: In Epidemiology, the prevalence of a disease in a statistical population is defined as the total cases of the disease in the population at any given time or the total number of cases in the population divided by the number of individuals in the population. It is used as an estimate of how a common condition is within a population over a certain period of time. It is also defined as a measure of morbidity which is the number of cases of a disease, infected persons, or condition present at a particular time in relation to the size of the population from which it is drawn. For example, the number of cases of Measles with in a population as of 1st July. It helps physicians or other health professionals understand the probability of certain diagnoses and are routinely used by Epidemiologists, health care providers, government agencies and insurers.</p>
<p>Prevalence rate is controlled by two elements:</p>
<p>i. The number of individuals who have been diseased in the past.
ii. The length of/ or duration of the illness.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-which-affect-prevalence-in-a-population">Factors which affect Prevalence in a Population<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-which-affect-prevalence-in-a-population" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prevalence rates are increased by:</p>
<p>i. Immigration of ill cases [in coming].
ii. Emigration of healthy persons [out going].</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>iii. Immigration of susceptible cases or those with the potential of becoming cases.
iv. Prolongation of life of cases in throat cure [increased duration of disease-chronicity]
v. Increased occurrences of new cases [increase in incidence]</p>
<p>Prevalence rates are increased by:
i. Immigration of healthy persons.
ii. Emigration of all cases.
iii. Improved cure rate of cases.
iv. Increased death rates from the diseases.
v. Decreased occurrences of diseases [incidence].
vi. Shorter duration of illnesses [acuteness]
vii. Death.</p>
<p>As new diseases develop in a population [as new cases rise] incidence goes up. As incidence goes up prevalence goes up.</p>
<p>The duration of disease affects prevalence: When disease has a long duration, prevalence remains higher and longer.</p>
<p>Intervention and treatment have effects on prevalence: as treatment reduces case, the duration of diseases and number of cases goes down – prevalence reduces.</p>
<ul>
<li>Immigration prevents new cases and reduces prevalence.</li>
<li>Prolongation of life of cases can increase duration and can add to the prevalence of chronic diseases.</li>
</ul>
<p>Prolongation of non-diseased and healthy population can reduce prevalence of acute diseases and and as healthy people increased, life expectancy increases.</p>

<p>=10 cases per 1,000.</p>
<p>There are four types of prevalence rates that are used by investigators:</p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Point Prevalence</strong>: This refers to the number of cases that occur during specified period of time, for example a year or the number of cases of individuals with a disease, condition, or illness at a single specific point in time divided by the number of existing cases at a point in time.</li>
</ol>
<p>Point prevalence therefore = <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mtext>Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;existing&nbsp;cases&nbsp;of&nbsp;the&nbsp;disease&nbsp;at&nbsp;a&nbsp;point&nbsp;in&nbsp;time</mtext><mo>×</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>000</mn></mrow><mtext>Total&nbsp;study&nbsp;population</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{\text{Number of existing cases of the disease at a point in time} \times 1,000}{\text{Total study population}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.4133em; vertical-align: -0.4811em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.9322em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Total&nbsp;study&nbsp;population</span></span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.4461em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;existing&nbsp;cases&nbsp;of&nbsp;the&nbsp;disease&nbsp;at&nbsp;a&nbsp;point&nbsp;in&nbsp;time</span></span><span class="mbin mtight">×</span><span class="mord mtight">1</span><span class="mpunct mtight">,</span><span class="mord mtight">000</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.4811em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p>Point prevalence measures the presence of a disease or a condition on a single short time point, theoretically stopping the clock for a minute, hour or day counting the existing cases of the disease.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The numerator includes all persons having the disease at a given moment irrespective of the length of time which has elapsed from the beginning of the illness to the time when prevalence is measured.</p>
<p>The denominator is the total population [affected and unaffected] within which the disease is present.</p>
<p>In contrast, incidence +rates measures events, while point prevalence rates measure what prevails or exists.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Period Prevalence: This is a measure that expresses the total number of cases of a disease known to have existed at some time during a specified period. It is the sum of point prevalence [the number of cases existing at the beginning of the period] and incidence [the number of case coming into existence during the period]i.e. period prevalence starts at the point in time and stops at a point in time.</li>
</ol>
<p>Period prevalence therefore= <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mtext>Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;existing&nbsp;cases&nbsp;of&nbsp;the&nbsp;disease&nbsp;within&nbsp;a&nbsp;period&nbsp;of&nbsp;time</mtext><mo>×</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>000</mn></mrow><mtext>Average&nbsp;study&nbsp;population</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{\text{Number of existing cases of the disease within a period of time} \times 1,000}{\text{Average study population}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.4133em; vertical-align: -0.4811em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.9322em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Average&nbsp;study&nbsp;population</span></span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.4461em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;existing&nbsp;cases&nbsp;of&nbsp;the&nbsp;disease&nbsp;within&nbsp;a&nbsp;period&nbsp;of&nbsp;time</span></span><span class="mbin mtight">×</span><span class="mord mtight">1</span><span class="mpunct mtight">,</span><span class="mord mtight">000</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.4811em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reflection-of-period-prevalence">Reflection of Period Prevalence:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reflection-of-period-prevalence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>i. All persons with disease that has been carried over from the previous time period or become ill at the end of the time period are included.
ii. New cases [incidence] occurring within the time period are included.
iii. Recurrences during a succeeding time period [usually one year] are included.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Life Time Prevalence: This refers to the total number of individuals that have had a condition, problem, or disease throughout their life, or at least major part of it. Life prevalence is considered as a form of period prevalence.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Annual Prevalence: The total number of people with a condition, problem, disease at any given time throughout the year. Cases of disease that started before the date of prevalence count but extended into study period are included in the annual prevalence rates. Those cases that started prior to the end of the study period and extended</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>throughout the year and/or had not yet recovered before the study period ended are counted.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="uses-of-prevalence-and-incidence-rates">Uses of Prevalence and Incidence Rates:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uses-of-prevalence-and-incidence-rates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Planning health services - public health services, medical services community health services.</li>
<li>Projecting medical care and need for hospital beds.</li>
<li>Research-epidemiological studies.</li>
<li>Predicting future.</li>
<li>Describing events of illness as they are.</li>
<li>Making comparisons.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="methods-used-in-epidemiology">METHODS USED IN EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#methods-used-in-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These include:</p>
<p>i. Collection of health related data,
ii. As a diagnostic tool for numerator data,
iii. For estimating the population at risk for the denominator data,</p>
<p>For recording data,</p>
<p>iv. For tabulation and analysis of data to introduce the essential information.
v. Use of information for decision making and evaluation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="unit-3">UNIT 3<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-3" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-primary-applications-of-epidemiology-in-public-health">The Primary Applications of Epidemiology in Public Health<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-primary-applications-of-epidemiology-in-public-health" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To set policy and plan programs, public health officials must assess the health of the population they serve and must determine whether health services are available, accessible, effective and efficient. Epidemiology provides data for directing public health action. The information is used when planning how to control and prevent disease in the community. Through public health surveillance, a health systematically collects, analyzes, interprets and disseminates health data on an ongoing basis. By knowing the ongoing pattern of disease occurrence and disease potential, a</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>health agency can effectively and efficiently investigate, prevent and control disease in the community.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="purposes-of-communicable-disease-investigation-and-general-principles-of-preventing-transmission">Purposes of Communicable Disease Investigation and General Principles of Preventing Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#purposes-of-communicable-disease-investigation-and-general-principles-of-preventing-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Introduction:</strong> These guidelines have been written to assist public health staff engaged in investigating communicable diseases. Their intent is to provide basic, practical, up-to-date and easy-to-understand information which has been coalesced and interpreted from a variety of sources. These guidelines have been written as carefully as possible to balance the amount of work necessary to follow up a reported case against the probability of controlling spread of the disease.</p>
<p>Only certain diseases are included in these guidelines. This is based upon their frequency and complexity. For further information about these and other diseases, consult Control of Communicable Diseases in Man.</p>
<p>Remember, <strong>these are only guidelines</strong>. All situations differ and best judgment should prevail. Some situations require a more stringent approach. Some require a less stringent approach.</p>
<p><strong>Purposes of Communicable Disease Investigation:</strong> Nothing is less self-fulfilling than going through the motions of a task without understanding its purpose. When investigating a report of communicable disease, always keeps in mind the purposes are to:</p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Prevent Transmission from Cases to Contacts:</strong> A full discussion of this topic begins below.</li>
<li><strong>Identify the Source of the Disease:</strong> The source may be in the environment, a food, or another person. Its identification can lead to control.</li>
<li><strong>Identify Other Cases:</strong> Each reported case should be regarded as a sentinel health event. Efforts to identify other cases which are not diagnosed and/or reported may uncover outbreaks and common sources. It is important to question the reported case about others who have been exposed or who have similar symptoms.</li>
<li><strong>Conduct Surveillance:</strong> Information collected when investigating a reported disease can be examined on a state or regional basis to identify outbreaks or trends and to formulate</li>
</ol>
<p>77</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>policy. This information is collected on standardized forms and often is sent to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention for surveillance on a national basis.</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Ensure that the Patient has Adequate Medical Supervision: Our responsibilities include making sure the patient is well cared for and understands the consequences of his illness. Where medical care is inadequate, appropriate referrals should be made.</li>
</ol>
<p>General Principles of Preventing Transmission: This section describes how cases and contacts can be managed to prevent transmission.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="case-management">Case Management:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#case-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Verify the Diagnosis: It is very important to promptly establish or verify the etiologic agent responsible for the disease. For many diseases reported by physicians or others, there are often little or no laboratory data initially available to verify the diagnosis. Since control efforts depend on the exact diagnosis, it is important to: (a) determine if this information exists; (b) arrange for laboratory tests if the information does not otherwise exist; or (c) make the best guess about diagnosis if it cannot be obtained.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Determine if the Case is Infectious or Possibly Infectious: If the case is still infectious, you must identify available measures for preventing transmission. There are two ways to assess the case's infectivity:</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>i. Estimation--uses the date of onset of illness, dates of known treatment, and known periods of infectiousness for an illness. For example, hepatitis A is no longer infectious after 1 week of the onset of symptoms. Strep throat is no longer infectious after 24 hours of treatment.</p>
<p>ii. Verification--requires laboratory testing of specimens for the case (for example, a stool specimen for a food handler with salmonellosis). Which method you use depends upon the disease (the period of communicability for some diseases is precise; for others it is not precise), the quality of information about the case (e.g., is date of onset known?), and the need to know definitely whether the case is infectious.</p>
<p>In practice, because of delays in diagnosis and reporting, many cases will have passed the infectious stage by the time they are reported. In this instance, the opportunity to prevent transmission is lost, but preventive efforts can be directed to the case's contacts, and other purposes of case investigation can be fulfilled. You may need to contact the physician to fill in information about the case,</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>determine results of laboratory tests, or arrange for tests. Also ask about similar cases for which a report may be pending.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Minimize the Duration of the Case's Infectivity: Drugs (antimicrobials) are the most important means to shorten the duration of infectivity for many diseases. Usually, decisions about drug treatment will be made by the time you investigate. However, sometimes it may be necessary to work with the patient and physician to ensure the patient is appropriately treated.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Put into Effect Practices for Preventing Transmission: These practices depend upon the disease and other circumstances. If the disease warrants, the case may have to employ these practices while ill and for a period after recovery during which (s)he is still infectious:</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>i. Disinfect and dispose of contaminated material (e.g., blood, saliva, feces, urine, eating utensils, bedding, clothes, toys, etc.)</p>
<p>ii. Disinfect the case, clothing, bedding, etc. (e.g., lice)</p>
<p>iii. Encourage behavioral practices of the case (e.g., hand washing, covering the nose and mouth when coughing/sneezing, and protecting lesions from contact with another person. Also, there may be a need for changes if individual are a food handler.)</p>
<p>iv. Isolate the case — the duration and degree depends upon the illness</p>
<p>Contact Management:</p>
<ol>
<li>Determine the risk of infection to contacts: What is the frequency, duration and dosage of the exposure? Often, these characteristics can only be described vaguely. It is usually reasonable to assume persons living in the same household had opportunity for exposure. Is the contact susceptible? Has the person been vaccinated, or previously infected? Is there evidence of a compromised immune system (surgery, steroids, etc.)? Does the disease result in immunity or are people generally susceptible?</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Determine if the contact is ill or infected: Look for clinical signs or symptoms. It is important to know if the contact is in the early stages of an illness or asymptomatic infected, you must rely on the laboratory. If you determine that a contact is ill or infected, manage him/her as a case.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Prevent illness in susceptible: It may be useful for you to think of contacts in two ways: Those who have been exposed, and those who might yet be exposed, either to the original source or to a contact who subsequently acquires an infection.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The choice of the preventive measure depends upon the disease. Rapid identification of susceptible is important. Remember, vaccines usually require a time period greater than one incubation period to develop antibody. Therefore, they rarely prevent illness in the first generation of contacts. They can, however prevent illness in later generations. Immune globulins must be given within a few days of contact to prevent or modify an infection, but they provide only short-term protection. Antimicrobials are particularly important for contacts exposed to strep, tuberculosis and meningitis.</p>
<p>Limit the activities of exposed persons — sometimes it may be prudent to recommend cessation of contact between the contact and the source. Another example is quarantining exposed persons to protect others not yet exposed.</p>
<p>Conduct personal surveillance of the exposed person — it may be important to call an exposed person by the end of the maximum incubation period to see if (s) he is ill (e.g., for hepatitis A), or simply ask the contact to call you if (s) he develops symptoms. The intensity of the surveillance depends upon the importance attached to preventing transmission from that person.</p>
<p>General Guidelines for Outbreak Investigations: The first action of a health agency when it receives a report of a case or a cluster of cases of a disease is to investigate. The investigation may be anything from a limited phone call to confirm reported information, or it may be as extensive as a field investigation coordinating the efforts of many people to determine the extent and cause of a large outbreak.</p>
<p>Outbreaks may be detected when routine, timely analysis of surveillance data reveals an increase in reported cases or an unusual clustering of cases. The weekly tabulation of disease reports by county surveillance officers are useful ways to monitor trends, evaluate geographical distribution throughout the county and identify possible clusters.</p>
<p>Why do we Investigate Outbreaks? The health agency has a responsibility to institute control and prevention measures. Outbreak investigations may be considered as opportunities to study disease events and are training exercises for those conducting the investigation. If cases are continuing to occur, the goal may be to prevent additional cases. If an outbreak appears to be almost over, the goal may be to prevent outbreaks in the future. The objective here would be to identify factors which contributed to the outbreak to implement measures that would prevent serious outbreaks in the future. Other concerns like public relations, political concerns and legal obligations may also need to be considered.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Steps in the Investigation:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Prepare for field work - talk to others with experience, review the literature.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Establish the existence of an outbreak - An outbreak or epidemic is the occurrence of more cases of disease than expected in a given area or among a specific group of people over a particular period of time. A cluster is an aggregation of cases in a given area over a particular period without regard to whether the number of cases is more than expected. Defining an epidemic requires that one determine what the expected number of cases would be usually from existing surveillance data. Some reasons for a "false" epidemic may be reports of sporadic cases of unrelated cases of the same disease, reports of similar cases but unrelated diseases, or changes in surveillance or reporting procedures.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Verify the diagnosis - Collect clinical information, contact the diagnosing physician, and confirm results of laboratory tests done.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Define and identify cases - Establish a case definition for the disease using a standard set of criteria for deciding whether an individual should be classified as having the health condition of interest.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Perform descriptive epidemiology - Collect information to characterize an outbreak by time, place and person. Remember the what, who, when and where of descriptive epidemiology.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Develop hypotheses - After collecting some of the initial information characterizing the outbreak, one should be able to formulate a hypothesis that addresses the source of the agent, the mode (and vehicle or vector) of transmission, and the exposures that caused the disease.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Evaluate hypotheses - Compare your hypothesis with the established facts. Is it plausible and consistent? If not, the hypothesis should be revised.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>81</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="8">
<li>
<p>As necessary, reconsider/refine hypotheses and execute additional studies.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Implement control and prevention measures - This is the primary goal of most outbreak investigations. Although mentioned far down on the list, prevention and control measures should be implemented as soon as possible in an outbreak setting. This may be accomplished by destroying contaminated foods, removing an infected food handler from the job, or immunizing and providing prophylaxis to a population at risk.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Communicate findings - An oral briefing or a written report are always useful to summarize an investigation. Formally presenting recommendations provides a blueprint for action and may serve in the health agency as a reference if the health agency encounters a similar situation in the future.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="data-collection-guidelines">Data Collection Guidelines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#data-collection-guidelines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Line listings. Traditionally we collect data on a standard case report form or questionnaire. A separate sheet of information from each person interviewed often makes synthesizing the data difficult. To create a line listing, critical items are selected from the data collection form and are organized so that data from multiple cases can be entered in columns on a single sheet to summarize the data. In a line listing, each column represents an important variable, such as name or identification number, age, sex, case classification, etc., while each row represents a different case.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The epidemic curve. The "epi" curve serves as a map of an epidemic. It provides a simple visual display of the outbreak's magnitude and time trend. Although simple it provides a great deal of information about an epidemic. You will usually be able to tell where you are in the time course of an epidemic, and what the future course might be. If you have identified the disease and know its usual incubation period, you usually can deduce a probable time period of exposure and can develop a questionnaire focusing on that time period. Finally, you may be able to draw inferences about the epidemic pattern — whether it is common source or propagated, or both. Drawing an epidemic curve, plotting time on the x axis and the number of cases on the y axis, will provide additional clues in furthering the investigation. Mapping of individual cases should be done by their date of onset. Changing the unit of time on the x axis may be necessary to best "see" the outbreak. This will depend on the incubation period of the disease you are dealing with.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="application-of-epidemiological-concepts-on-health-services">Application of Epidemiological Concepts on Health Services:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#application-of-epidemiological-concepts-on-health-services" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>It is used in the investigation of diseases [communicable and non-communicable], problems, situations and events that have affected or are affecting or will be affecting health of the organism in our environment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is a basic tool for looking for signs and symptoms in a community health.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used in the elucidation [An act of explaining that serves to clear up and cast light on or an interpretation that removes obstacles to understanding] of causal mechanisms, the explanation of local health characteristics of disease occurrence and administrative guidance in the provision of health services.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used in preventive interventions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to determine fertility in a population.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to determine the number of accidents and their causal mechanisms.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to study the health history of a population.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to diagnose the health of a community and measures of ill health [A state in which you are unable to function normally and without pain] in terms of incidence, prevalence, and mortality from a defined disease.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to study the working and utilization of health services.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to estimate the group experience; what are the individual’s risk on the average of disease incidence and defects.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to complete a clinical picture of chronic disease and describe its natural history [The scientific study of plants or animals (more observational than experimental) usually published in popular magazines rather than in academic journals].</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to identify syndromes [A pattern of symptoms indicative of some disease] by describing the distribution, association and disassociation of phenomena [Any state or process known through the senses rather than by intuition or reasoning or a remarkable development] on the population.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used to search for cases of health and disease by study the incidence in different groups to estimate the relative importance of different causes in multiple etiologies [The philosophical study of causation].</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It can be used as strategy [An elaborate and systematic plan of action] for studying man and his total environment.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>83</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="15">
<li>It can be used to develop knowledge about both the earliest and beginnings of dysfunction and the characteristics of individuals within the groups that predispose them to futuristic problems.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="uses-of-epidemiologic-studies">Uses of Epidemiologic studies:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uses-of-epidemiologic-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are periodic or continuing assessment of the health status of the community.</p>
<p>i. Periodic or continuing evaluation of the effectiveness of health services.
ii. Surveillance of specific diseases.
iii. Investigation and containment of disease out-breaks.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="uses-of-epidemiologic-health-surveys">Uses of Epidemiologic Health Surveys:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uses-of-epidemiologic-health-surveys" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>i. To measure the total amount of illness in the population.
ii. To measure the amount of illness caused by a specific disease.
iii. To study the nutritional status of the population.
iv. To examine the utilization of existing health care facilities and demand for new ones.
v. To measure the distribution in the population of a particular characteristic e.g. sources of water.
vi. To examine the role and relationship of one or more factors in etiology of disease.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-2f2e5beb.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg"></p>
<p>Simplified causal web linking exposures and outcomes for a discussion of the issues involved in quantification of risks within this framework.</p>
<p>Random: Lacking any definite plan or order or purpose; governed by or depending on chance</p>
<p>Random Sample: a sample in which every element in the population has an equal chance of being selected</p>
<p>Sampling Methods: These are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Random sampling: The selection of a random sample; each element of the population has an equal chance of been selected</li>
<li>Random sampling method: A sample may be defined as random if every individual in the population being sampled has an equal likelihood of being included in the sample. Random selection is the basis of all good sampling techniques. Selection of a sample entails identification of all the individuals from whom the selection will be made. Then make a list of the study population from whom to select the sample. You may also assign a number to each individual and then select certain numbers by reference to random sample. The starting point for selection should be randomly chosen.</li>
<li>Systematic Sampling: Here every nth is selected from the list or from some other ordering. A systematic sample can be drawn from patients ordered according to the time of their attendance, admission or discharge from a hospital. This enables a sample to be drawn without initial listing of all people among whom the selection will</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>85</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>be made. This method has major advantage over a simple random sample. The starting point for selection should be randomly chosen.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>Multi-stage sampling method: In a survey covering a large area such as a district, province or country, an initial sample may be taken from units of population such as village or schools. The villages or schools are listed and a random sample of the required number is selected. Then a list of the individuals within the chosen villages is made and a sample taken from them. This is a two - stage sampling that is used for such surveys as B.C.G. scar and tuberculin test surveys. This procedure of subdividing population into two smaller units may be extended to two or more stages as required. Listing is restricted to the smaller units from whom the final selection is made. In this case, a village or a school is a cluster sample as the individuals selected are clustered in the same village/school. The method may cause error if the variable attribute or disease is itself clustered in the population e.g. Onchocerciasis clustering in the village near the streams where simulium flies breed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Stratified Sampling/ proportional sampling/ representative sampling: The population is divided into subpopulations (strata) and random samples are taken of each stratum. If a disease is unevenly distributed within a population in respect to sex, age, etc a stratified sample is used.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Choice of Sampling Method: The choice of a particular sampling method is largely determined by practical consideration. In field work it is almost impossible to select a sample that is fully random. To check a bias that is always in every sample, the investigator must have some basic knowledge of the study population. For example, prevalence of trachoma cannot be determined solely by examining a sample of people attending health center.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Control Groups: These are groups of subjects in analytical epidemiological surveys whereby the study group with a disease is compared with those without the disease or condition.</p>
<p>Examples:</p>
<p>i. In case-control study of the association between a causation and disease.</p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>ii. In representative survey, the incidence of the disease among persons exposed to the causative factor can be compared with those persons not exposed.</p>
<p>iii. The ideal control group is one which is exactly the same as the study group in all respects except for the characteristics which are to be studied. Controls can be selected from the general population or patients with one or more other diseases.</p>
<p><strong>Attack Rate</strong>: Attack rates are cumulative incidence rates and are used in epidemics. These rates refer to the number of persons ill from a specific disease in a limited time period in a specific group i.e. they are computed based on limited time exposure and risk and the limited group of people at risk.</p>
<p>Sometimes the term infection rate shows the incidence of all persons with signs and symptoms of disease and also includes the unapparent cases of infections.</p>
<p>Attack rates are commonly used in food poisoning situations or chemical exposure in a group of workers.</p>
<p>In cases of food poisoning, food sanitation preparation measures vary and likely food borne illness due to bacteria occur among those who might have eaten a particular food. In carrying out an investigation of an out-break of this nature, one need to look at:</p>
<ol>
<li>Various food sources.</li>
<li>Who prepared what food?</li>
<li>Cases of ill people verses well people.</li>
<li>Who eats what when and how much?</li>
</ol>
<p>From the results of the investigation, attack rates are calculated.</p>
<p>The epidemiological concept here is to determine whether there is an association between the risk of exposure [eating the food], at specific event and the specific illness.</p>
<p>Attack rate therefore is number of persons developing the disease within the range of the incubation period within a total population exposed to the risk factor.</p>

<p>Number of people attending the event [exposed]</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mtext>General&nbsp;attack&nbsp;rate</mtext><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mrow><mtext>Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;people&nbsp;ill&nbsp;[cases]&nbsp;within&nbsp;the&nbsp;time&nbsp;period</mtext><mo>×</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>000</mn></mrow><mtext>Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;people&nbsp;at&nbsp;risk&nbsp;within&nbsp;the&nbsp;time&nbsp;period</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\text{General attack rate} = \frac{\text{Number of people ill [cases] within the time period} \times 1,000}{\text{Number of people at risk within the time period}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">General&nbsp;attack&nbsp;rate</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.4911em; <p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mtext>Food&nbsp;specific&nbsp;attack&nbsp;rate</mtext><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mrow><mtext>Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;people&nbsp;who&nbsp;ate&nbsp;a&nbsp;specific&nbsp;food&nbsp;and&nbsp;became&nbsp;ill</mtext><mo>×</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>000</mn></mrow><mtext>Total&nbsp;number&nbsp;of&nbsp;people&nbsp;who&nbsp;ate&nbsp;the&nbsp;food</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\text{Food specific attack rate} = \frac{\text{Number of people who ate a specific food and became ill} \times 1,000}{\text{Total number of people who ate the food}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.4811em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.9322em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Total&nbsp;number&nbsp;of&nbsp;people&nbsp;who&nbsp;ate&nbsp;the&nbsp;food</span></span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.4461em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;people&nbsp;who&nbsp;ate&nbsp;a&nbsp;specific&nbsp;food&nbsp;and&nbsp;became&nbsp;ill</span></span><span class="mbin mtight">×</span><span class="mord mtight">1</span><span class="mpunct mtight">,</span><span class="mord mtight">000</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.4811em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p>Attack rates are determine from the broad perspective [crude attack rate] downward to narrow focus [attack rate], and then finally a very specific assessment is conducted [food – specific attack rate]</p>
<p><strong>Primary and Secondary Attack:</strong> This refers to number of persons who had contact or exposure to the primary infectious person or the primary source of infection within the incubation period of the pathogen.</p>
<p>The assessment uses the total number of exposed cases of exposed cases i.e. the number of infected persons per number susceptible exposed persons multiplied by 1,000.</p>
<p><strong>Note:</strong> the denominator excludes individuals who have previously had the disease and are now immune of susceptibility exposed persons multiplied by 1,000.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mtext>Secondary&nbsp;attack&nbsp;rate</mtext><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mrow><mtext>Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;exposed&nbsp;persons&nbsp;developing&nbsp;the&nbsp;disease&nbsp;within&nbsp;incubation&nbsp;period</mtext><mo>×</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>000</mn></mrow><mtext>Total&nbsp;number&nbsp;of&nbsp;persons&nbsp;exposed&nbsp;to&nbsp;the&nbsp;primary&nbsp;case</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\text{Secondary attack rate} = \frac{\text{Number of exposed persons developing the disease within incubation period} \times 1,000}{\text{Total number of persons exposed to the primary case}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">Secondary&nbsp;attack&nbsp;rate</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.4133em; vertical-align: -0.4811em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.9322em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Total&nbsp;number&nbsp;of&nbsp;persons&nbsp;exposed&nbsp;to&nbsp;the&nbsp;primary&nbsp;case</span></span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.4461em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;exposed&nbsp;persons&nbsp;developing&nbsp;the&nbsp;disease&nbsp;within&nbsp;incubation&nbsp;period</span></span><span class="mbin mtight">×</span><span class="mord mtight">1</span><span class="mpunct mtight">,</span><span class="mord mtight">000</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.4811em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p><strong>Case Fatality:</strong> A number of people will die from those with the disease over a period of time.</p>
<p><strong>Mortality:</strong> This measure tells what proportion of the entire population die yearly from the disease.</p>
<p><strong>Significance:</strong> measures the spread of infection within a family or household following exposure to the first or primary case in the family.</p>
<p>The numerator is the number of cases and the denominator consists of all persons who are exposed to the risk.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mtext>Significance</mtext><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mtext>Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;cases</mtext><mtext>All&nbsp;persons&nbsp;who&nbsp;are&nbsp;exposed&nbsp;to&nbsp;the&nbsp;risk&nbsp;factor</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\text{Significance} = \frac{\text{Number of cases}}{\text{All persons who are exposed to the risk factor}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">Significance</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.3612em; vertical-align: -0.4811em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8801em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">All&nbsp;persons&nbsp;who&nbsp;are&nbsp;exposed&nbsp;to&nbsp;the&nbsp;risk&nbsp;factor</span></span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.394em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">Number&nbsp;of&nbsp;cases</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.4811em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Assessment of Accuracy of Validity: Two indices are used to evaluate the accuracy of a test – those of the sensitivity and specificity. These indices are usually determined by administering the test to one group of persons who have the disease and to another group who do not and then comparing the results.</p>
<p>Those testing positive have the disease and are called “true positive”, those testing positive and do not have the disease are called “false positive”.</p>
<p>Those testing negative and have the disease are called “false negative”, and those testing negative and do not have the disease are called “true negatives”.</p>
<p>Sensitivity: is defined as the percent of those who have the disease and are so indicated by the test.</p>
<p>Specificity: is defined as the percent of those who do not have the disease and are indicated by the test.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-epidemiological-studies">Types of Epidemiological Studies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-epidemiological-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Case series: Case-series may refer to the qualitative study of the experience of a single patient, or small group of patients with a similar diagnosis, or to a statistical technique comparing periods during which patients are exposed to some factor with the potential to produce illness with periods when they are unexposed.</li>
</ol>
<p>Using the data from the series, analytic studies could be done to investigate possible causal factors. These can include case control studies or prospective studies. A case control study would involve matching comparable controls without the disease to the cases in the series. A prospective study would involve following the case series over time to evaluate the disease's natural history.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Case control studies: Case control studies select subjects based on their disease status. A group of individuals that are disease positive (the "case" group) is compared with a group of disease negative individuals (the "control" group). The control group should ideally come from the same population that gave rise to the cases. The case control study looks back through time at potential exposures that both groups (cases and controls) may have encountered. A <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">2 \times 2</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span></span></span></span></span> table is constructed; displaying exposed cases (A), exposed controls (B), unexposed cases (C) and unexposed controls (D). The statistic generated to measure association is the odds ratio (OR), which is the ratio of the odds of exposure in the cases (A/C) to the odds of exposure in the controls (B/D), i.e. <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">R</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi></mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{OR} = (\mathrm{AD} / \mathrm{BC})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">OR</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">AD</span></span><span class="mord">/</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">BC</span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="case-controls">Case Controls<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#case-controls" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prepared by O.E. Akoth</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
